"Well, Prince, so Genoa and Lucca are now just family estates of the Buonapartes.

But I warn you, if you don't tell me that this means war, if you still try to defend the infamies and horrors perpetrated by that Antichrist     I really belie e he is Antichrist     I will ha e nothing more to do with you and you are no longer my friend, no longer my 'faithful sla e,' as you call yourself!

But how do you do?

I see I ha e frightened you sit down and tell me all the news."

It was in July, 1805, and the speaker was the well known Anna Pa lo na Scherer, maid of honor and fa orite of the Empress Marya Fedoro na. With these words she greeted Prince  asili Kuragin, a man of high rank and importance, who was the first to arri e at her reception.

Anna Pa lo na had had a cough for some days. She was, as she said, suffering from la grippe  grippe being then a new word in St. Petersburg, used only by the elite.

All her in itations without exception, written in French, and deli ered by a scarlet li eried footman that morning, ran as follows 

"If you ha e nothing better to do, Count (or Prince), and if the prospect of spending an e ening with a poor in alid is not too terrible, I shall be  ery charmed to see you tonight between 7 and 10     Annette Scherer."

"Hea ens! what a  irulent attack!" replied the prince, not in the least disconcerted by this reception.

He had just entered, wearing an embroidered court uniform, knee breeches, and shoes, and had stars on his breast and a serene expression on his flat face.

He spoke in that refined French in which our grandfathers not only spoke but thought, and with the gentle, patronizing intonation natural to a man of importance who had grown old in society and at court.

He went up to Anna Pa lo na, kissed her hand, presenting to her his bald, scented, and shining head, and complacently seated himself on the sofa.

"First of all, dear friend, tell me how you are. Set your friend's mind at rest," said he without altering his tone, beneath the politeness and affected sympathy of which indifference and e en irony could be discerned.

"Can one be well while suffering morally?

Can one be calm in times like these if one has any feeling?" said Anna Pa lo na. "You are staying the whole e ening, I hope?"

"And the fete at the English ambassador's?

Today is Wednesday.

I must put in an appearance there," said the prince. "My daughter is coming for me to take me there."

"I thought today's fete had been canceled.

I confess all these festi ities and fireworks are becoming wearisome."

"If they had known that you wished it, the entertainment would ha e been put off," said the prince, who, like a wound up clock, by force of habit said things he did not e en wish to be belie ed.

"Don't tease!

Well, and what has been decided about No osiltse 's dispatch?

You know e erything."

"What can one say about it?" replied the prince in a cold, listless tone.

"What has been decided?

They ha e decided that Buonaparte has burnt his boats, and I belie e that we are ready to burn ours." Prince  asili always spoke languidly, like an actor repeating a stale part.

Anna Pa lo na Scherer on the contrary, despite her forty years, o erflowed with animation and impulsi eness.

To be an enthusiast had become her social  ocation and, sometimes e en when she did not feel like it, she became enthusiastic in order not to disappoint the expectations of those who knew her.

The subdued smile which, though it did not suit her faded features, always played round her lips expressed, as in a spoiled child, a continual consciousness of her charming defect, which she neither wished, nor could, nor considered it necessary, to correct.

In the midst of a con ersation on political matters Anna Pa lo na burst out 

"Oh, don't speak to me of Austria.

Perhaps I don't understand things, but Austria ne er has wished, and does not wish, for war.

She is betraying us!

Russia alone must sa e Europe.

Our gracious so ereign recognizes his high  ocation and will be true to it.

That is the one thing I ha e faith in!

Our good and wonderful so ereign has to perform the noblest role on earth, and he is so  irtuous and noble that God will not forsake him. He will fulfill his  ocation and crush the hydra of re olution, which has become more terrible than e er in the person of this murderer and  illain!

We alone must a enge the blood of the just one.... Whom, I ask you, can we rely on?... England with her commercial spirit will not and cannot understand the Emperor Alexander's loftiness of soul.

She has refused to e acuate Malta.

She wanted to find, and still seeks, some secret moti e in our actions.

What answer did No osiltse  get? None.

The English ha e not understood and cannot understand the self abnegation of our Emperor who wants nothing for himself, but only desires the good of mankind.

And what ha e they promised?

Nothing!

And what little they ha e promised they will not perform!

Prussia has always declared that Buonaparte is in incible, and that all Europe is powerless before him.... And I don't belie e a word that Hardenburg says, or Haugwitz either.

This famous Prussian neutrality is just a trap. I ha e faith only in God and the lofty destiny of our adored monarch.

He will sa e Europe!" She suddenly paused, smiling at her own impetuosity.

"I think," said the prince with a smile, "that if you had been sent instead of our dear Wintzingerode you would ha e captured the King of Prussia's consent by assault.

You are so eloquent.

Will you gi e me a cup of tea?"

"In a moment.

A propos," she added, becoming calm again, "I am expecting two  ery interesting men tonight, le  icomte de Mortemart, who is connected with the Montmorencys through the Rohans, one of the best French families.

He is one of the genuine emigres, the good ones.

And also the Abbe Morio. Do you know that profound thinker?

He has been recei ed by the Emperor.

Had you heard?"

"I shall be delighted to meet them," said the prince. "But tell me," he added with studied carelessness as if it had only just occurred to him, though the question he was about to ask was the chief moti e of his  isit, "is it true that the Dowager Empress wants Baron Funke to be appointed first secretary at  ienna?

The baron by all accounts is a poor creature." Prince  asili wished to obtain this post for his son, but others were trying through the Dowager Empress Marya Fedoro na to secure it for the baron.

Anna Pa lo na almost closed her eyes to indicate that neither she nor anyone else had a right to criticize what the Empress desired or was pleased with.

"Baron Funke has been recommended to the Dowager Empress by her sister," was all she said, in a dry and mournful tone.

As she named the Empress, Anna Pa lo na's face suddenly assumed an expression of profound and sincere de otion and respect mingled with sadness, and this occurred e ery time she mentioned her illustrious patroness.

She added that Her Majesty had deigned to show Baron Funke beaucoup d'estime, and again her face clouded o er with sadness.

The prince was silent and looked indifferent.

But, with the womanly and courtierlike quickness and tact habitual to her, Anna Pa lo na wished both to rebuke him (for daring to speak he had done of a man recommended to the Empress) and at the same time to console him, so she said 

"Now about your family. Do you know that since your daughter came out e eryone has been enraptured by her?

They say she is amazingly beautiful."

The prince bowed to signify his respect and gratitude.

"I often think," she continued after a short pause, drawing nearer to the prince and smiling amiably at him as if to show that political and social topics were ended and the time had come for intimate con ersation     "I often think how unfairly sometimes the joys of life are distributed.

Why has fate gi en you two such splendid children? I don't speak of Anatole, your youngest. I don't like him," she added in a tone admitting of no rejoinder and raising her eyebrows. "Two such charming children.

And really you appreciate them less than anyone, and so you don't deser e to ha e them."

And she smiled her ecstatic smile.

"I can't help it," said the prince.

"La ater would ha e said I lack the bump of paternity."

"Don't joke  I mean to ha e a serious talk with you.

Do you know I am dissatisfied with your younger son?

Between oursel es" (and her face assumed its melancholy expression), "he was mentioned at Her Majesty's and you were pitied...."

The prince answered nothing, but she looked at him significantly, awaiting a reply.

He frowned.

"What would you ha e me do?" he said at last. "You know I did all a father could for their education, and they ha e both turned out fools. Hippolyte is at least a quiet fool, but Anatole is an acti e one.

That is the only difference between them." He said this smiling in a way more natural and animated than usual, so that the wrinkles round his mouth  ery clearly re ealed something unexpectedly coarse and unpleasant.

"And why are children born to such men as you?

If you were not a father there would be nothing I could reproach you with," said Anna Pa lo na, looking up pensi ely.

"I am your faithful sla e and to you alone I can confess that my children are the bane of my life. It is the cross I ha e to bear. That is how I explain it to myself. It can't be helped!" He said no more, but expressed his resignation to cruel fate by a gesture.

Anna Pa lo na meditated.

"Ha e you ne er thought of marrying your prodigal son Anatole?" she asked.

"They say old maids ha e a mania for matchmaking, and though I don't feel that weakness in myself as yet, I know a little person who is  ery unhappy with her father. She is a relation of yours, Princess Mary Bolkonskaya." Prince  asili did not reply, though, with the quickness of memory and perception befitting a man of the world, he indicated by a mo ement of the head that he was considering this information.

"Do you know," he said at last, e idently unable to check the sad current of his thoughts, "that Anatole is costing me forty thousand rubles a year? And," he went on after a pause, "what will it be in fi e years, if he goes on like this?"

Presently he added 

"That's what we fathers ha e to put up with....

Is this princess of yours rich?"

"Her father is  ery rich and stingy.

He li es in the country.

He is the well known Prince Bolkonski who had to retire from the army under the late Emperor, and was nicknamed 'the King of Prussia.'

He is  ery cle er but eccentric, and a bore.

The poor girl is  ery unhappy. She has a brother  I think you know him, he married Lise Meinen lately.

He is an aide de camp of Kutuzo 's and will be here tonight."

"Listen, dear Annette," said the prince, suddenly taking Anna Pa lo na's hand and for some reason drawing it downwards. "Arrange that affair for me and I shall always be your most de oted sla e slafe with an f, as a  illage elder of mine writes in his reports.

She is rich and of good family and that's all I want."

And with the familiarity and easy grace peculiar to him, he raised the maid of honor's hand to his lips, kissed it, and swung it to and fro as he lay back in his armchair, looking in another direction.

"Attendez," said Anna Pa lo na, reflecting, "I'll speak to Lise, young Bolkonski's wife, this  ery e ening, and perhaps the thing can be arranged.

It shall be on your family's behalf that I'll start my apprenticeship as old maid."

CHAPTER II

Anna Pa lo na's drawing room was gradually filling.

The highest Petersburg society was assembled there  people differing widely in age and character but alike in the social circle to which they belonged. Prince  asili's daughter, the beautiful Helene, came to take her father to the ambassador's entertainment  she wore a ball dress and her badge as maid of honor.

The youthful little Princess Bolkonskaya, known as la femme la plus seduisante de Petersbourg,   was also there. She had been married during the pre ious winter, and being pregnant did not go to any large gatherings, but only to small receptions.

Prince  asili's son, Hippolyte, had come with Mortemart, whom he introduced. The Abbe Morio and many others had also come.

  The most fascinating woman in Petersburg. To each new arri al Anna Pa lo na said, "You ha e not yet seen my aunt," or "You do not know my aunt?" and  ery gra ely conducted him or her to a little old lady, wearing large bows of ribbon in her cap, who had come sailing in from another room as soon as the guests began to arri e  and slowly turning her eyes from the  isitor to her aunt, Anna Pa lo na mentioned each one's name and then left them.

Each  isitor performed the ceremony of greeting this old aunt whom not one of them knew, not one of them wanted to know, and not one of them cared about  Anna Pa lo na obser ed these greetings with mournful and solemn interest and silent appro al.

The aunt spoke to each of them in the same words, about their health and her own, and the health of Her Majesty, "who, thank God, was better today."

And each  isitor, though politeness pre ented his showing impatience, left the old woman with a sense of relief at ha ing performed a  exatious duty and did not return to her the whole e ening.

The young Princess Bolkonskaya had brought some work in a gold embroidered  el et bag.

Her pretty little upper lip, on which a delicate dark down was just perceptible, was too short for her teeth, but it lifted all the more sweetly, and was especially charming when she occasionally drew it down to meet the lower lip.

As is always the case with a thoroughly attracti e woman, her defect     the shortness of her upper lip and her half open mouth     seemed to be her own special and peculiar form of beauty.

E eryone brightened at the sight of this pretty young woman, so soon to become a mother, so full of life and health, and carrying her burden so lightly.

Old men and dull dispirited young ones who looked at her, after being in her company and talking to her a little while, felt as if they too were becoming, like her, full of life and health.

All who talked to her, and at each word saw her bright smile and the constant gleam of her white teeth, thought that they were in a specially amiable mood that day.

The little princess went round the table with quick, short, swaying steps, her workbag on her arm, and gaily spreading out her dress sat down on a sofa near the sil er samo ar, as if all she was doing was a pleasure to herself and to all around her.

"I ha e brought my work," said she in French, displaying her bag and addressing all present.

"Mind, Annette, I hope you ha e not played a wicked trick on me," she added, turning to her hostess. "You wrote that it was to be quite a small reception, and just see how badly I am dressed."

And she spread out her arms to show her short waisted, lace trimmed, dainty gray dress, girdled with a broad ribbon just below the breast.

"Soyez tranquille, Lise, you will always be prettier than anyone else," replied Anna Pa lo na.

"You know," said the princess in the same tone of  oice and still in French, turning to a general, "my husband is deserting me?

He is going to get himself killed.

Tell me what this wretched war is for?" she added, addressing Prince  asili, and without waiting for an answer she turned to speak to his daughter, the beautiful Helene.

"What a delightful woman this little princess is!" said Prince  asili to Anna Pa lo na.

One of the next arri als was a stout, hea ily built young man with close cropped hair, spectacles, the light colored breeches fashionable at that time, a  ery high ruffle, and a brown dress coat.

This stout young man was an illegitimate son of Count Bezukho , a well known grandee of Catherine's time who now lay dying in Moscow.

The young man had not yet entered either the military or ci il ser ice, as he had only just returned from abroad where he had been educated, and this was his first appearance in society.

Anna Pa lo na greeted him with the nod she accorded to the lowest hierarchy in her drawing room.

But in spite of this lowest grade greeting, a look of anxiety and fear, as at the sight of something too large and unsuited to the place, came o er her face when she saw Pierre enter.

Though he was certainly rather bigger than the other men in the room, her anxiety could only ha e reference to the cle er though shy, but obser ant and natural, expression which distinguished him from e eryone else in that drawing room.

"It is  ery good of you, Monsieur Pierre, to come and  isit a poor in alid," said Anna Pa lo na, exchanging an alarmed glance with her aunt as she conducted him to her.

Pierre murmured something unintelligible, and continued to look round as if in search of something.

On his way to the aunt he bowed to the little princess with a pleased smile, as to an intimate acquaintance.

Anna Pa lo na's alarm was justified, for Pierre turned away from the aunt without waiting to hear her speech about Her Majesty's health.

Anna Pa lo na in dismay detained him with the words 

"Do you know the Abbe Morio? He is a most interesting man."

"Yes, I ha e heard of his scheme for perpetual peace, and it is  ery interesting but hardly feasible."

"You think so?" rejoined Anna Pa lo na in order to say something and get away to attend to her duties as hostess. But Pierre now committed a re erse act of impoliteness.

First he had left a lady before she had finished speaking to him, and now he continued to speak to another who wished to get away.

With his head bent, and his big feet spread apart, he began explaining his reasons for thinking the abbe's plan chimerical.

"We will talk of it later," said Anna Pa lo na with a smile.

And ha ing got rid of this young man who did not know how to beha e, she resumed her duties as hostess and continued to listen and watch, ready to help at any point where the con ersation might happen to flag.

As the foreman of a spinning mill, when he has set the hands to work, goes round and notices here a spindle that has stopped or there one that creaks or makes more noise than it should, and hastens to check the machine or set it in proper motion, so Anna Pa lo na mo ed about her drawing room, approaching now a silent, now a too noisy group, and by a word or slight rearrangement kept the con ersational machine in steady, proper, and regular motion.

But amid these cares her anxiety about Pierre was e ident.

She kept an anxious watch on him when he approached the group round Mortemart to listen to what was being said there, and again when he passed to another group whose center was the abbe.

Pierre had been educated abroad, and this reception at Anna Pa lo na's was the first he had attended in Russia.

He knew that all the intellectual lights of Petersburg were gathered there and, like a child in a toyshop, did not know which way to look, afraid of missing any cle er con ersation that was to be heard.

Seeing the self confident and refined expression on the faces of those present he was always expecting to hear something  ery profound.

At last he came up to Morio.

Here the con ersation seemed interesting and he stood waiting for an opportunity to express his own  iews, as young people are fond of doing.

CHAPTER III

Anna Pa lo na's reception was in full swing.

The spindles hummed steadily and ceaselessly on all sides.

With the exception of the aunt, beside whom sat only one elderly lady, who with her thin careworn face was rather out of place in this brilliant society, the whole company had settled into three groups.

One, chiefly masculine, had formed round the abbe. Another, of young people, was grouped round the beautiful Princess Helene, Prince  asili's daughter, and the little Princess Bolkonskaya,  ery pretty and rosy, though rather too plump for her age.

The third group was gathered round Mortemart and Anna Pa lo na.

The  icomte was a nice looking young man with soft features and polished manners, who e idently considered himself a celebrity but out of politeness modestly placed himself at the disposal of the circle in which he found himself.

Anna Pa lo na was ob iously ser ing him up as a treat to her guests.

As a cle er maitre d'hotel ser es up as a specially choice delicacy a piece of meat that no one who had seen it in the kitchen would ha e cared to eat, so Anna Pa lo na ser ed up to her guests, first the  icomte and then the abbe, as peculiarly choice morsels.

The group about Mortemart immediately began discussing the murder of the Duc d'Enghien.

The  icomte said that the Duc d'Enghien had perished by his own magnanimity, and that there were particular reasons for Buonaparte's hatred of him.

"Ah, yes!

Do tell us all about it,  icomte," said Anna Pa lo na, with a pleasant feeling that there was something a la Louis X  in the sound of that sentence  "Contez nous cela,  icomte."

The  icomte bowed and smiled courteously in token of his willingness to comply.

Anna Pa lo na arranged a group round him, in iting e eryone to listen to his tale.

"The  icomte knew the duc personally," whispered Anna Pa lo na to of the guests. "The  icomte is a wonderful raconteur," said she to another. "How e idently he belongs to the best society," said she to a third  and the  icomte was ser ed up to the company in the choicest and most ad antageous style, like a well garnished joint of roast beef on a hot dish.

The  icomte wished to begin his story and ga e a subtle smile.

"Come o er here, Helene, dear," said Anna Pa lo na to the beautiful young princess who was sitting some way off, the center of another group.

The princess smiled. She rose with the same unchanging smile with which she had first entered the room     the smile of a perfectly beautiful woman.

With a slight rustle of her white dress trimmed with moss and i y, with a gleam of white shoulders, glossy hair, and sparkling diamonds, she passed between the men who made way for her, not looking at any of them but smiling on all, as if graciously allowing each the pri ilege of admiring her beautiful figure and shapely shoulders, back, and bosom     which in the fashion of those days were  ery much exposed     and she seemed to bring the glamour of a ballroom with her as she mo ed toward Anna Pa lo na.

Helene was so lo ely that not only did she not show any trace of coquetry, but on the contrary she e en appeared shy of her unquestionable and all too  ictorious beauty.

She seemed to wish, but to be unable, to diminish its effect.

"How lo ely!" said e eryone who saw her  and the  icomte lifted his shoulders and dropped his eyes as if startled by something extraordinary when she took her seat opposite and beamed upon him also with her unchanging smile.

"Madame, I doubt my ability before such an audience," said he, smilingly inclining his head.

The princess rested her bare round arm on a little table and considered a reply unnecessary.

She smilingly waited.

All the time the story was being told she sat upright, glancing now at her beautiful round arm, altered in shape by its pressure on the table, now at her still more beautiful bosom, on which she readjusted a diamond necklace. From time to time she smoothed the folds of her dress, and whene er the story produced an effect she glanced at Anna Pa lo na, at once adopted just the expression she saw on the maid of honor's face, and again relapsed into her radiant smile.

The little princess had also left the tea table and followed Helene.

"Wait a moment, I'll get my work.... Now then, what are you thinking of?" she went on, turning to Prince Hippolyte.

"Fetch me my workbag."

There was a general mo ement as the princess, smiling and talking merrily to e eryone at once, sat down and gaily arranged herself in her seat.

"Now I am all right," she said, and asking the  icomte to begin, she took up her work.

Prince Hippolyte, ha ing brought the workbag, joined the circle and mo ing a chair close to hers seated himself beside her.

Le charmant Hippolyte was surprising by his extraordinary resemblance to his beautiful sister, but yet more by the fact that in spite of this resemblance he was exceedingly ugly.

His features were like his sister's, but while in her case e erything was lit up by a joyous, self satisfied, youthful, and constant smile of animation, and by the wonderful classic beauty of her figure, his face on the contrary was dulled by imbecility and a constant expression of sullen self confidence, while his body was thin and weak.

His eyes, nose, and mouth all seemed puckered into a  acant, wearied grimace, and his arms and legs always fell into unnatural positions.

"It's not going to be a ghost story?" said he, sitting down beside the princess and hastily adjusting his lorgnette, as if without this instrument he could not begin to speak.

"Why no, my dear fellow," said the astonished narrator, shrugging his shoulders.

"Because I hate ghost stories," said Prince Hippolyte in a tone which showed that he only understood the meaning of his words after he had uttered them.

He spoke with such self confidence that his hearers could not be sure whether what he said was  ery witty or  ery stupid.

He was dressed in a dark green dress coat, knee breeches of the color of cuisse de nymphe effrayee, as he called it, shoes, and silk stockings.

The  icomte told his tale  ery neatly. It was an anecdote, then current, to the effect that the Duc d'Enghien had gone secretly to Paris to  isit Mademoiselle George  that at her house he came upon Bonaparte, who also enjoyed the famous actress' fa ors, and that in his presence Napoleon happened to fall into one of the fainting fits to which he was subject, and was thus at the duc's mercy. The latter spared him, and this magnanimity Bonaparte subsequently repaid by death.

The story was  ery pretty and interesting, especially at the point where the ri als suddenly recognized one another  and the ladies looked agitated.

"Charming!" said Anna Pa lo na with an inquiring glance at the little princess.

"Charming!" whispered the little princess, sticking the needle into her work as if to testify that the interest and fascination of the story pre ented her from going on with it.

The  icomte appreciated this silent praise and smiling gratefully prepared to continue, but just then Anna Pa lo na, who had kept a watchful eye on the young man who so alarmed her, noticed that he was talking too loudly and  ehemently with the abbe, so she hurried to the rescue.

Pierre had managed to start a con ersation with the abbe about the balance of power, and the latter, e idently interested by the young man's simple minded eagerness, was explaining his pet theory.

Both were talking and listening too eagerly and too naturally, which was why Anna Pa lo na disappro ed.

"The means are... the balance of power in Europe and the rights of the people," the abbe was saying. "It is only necessary for one powerful nation like Russia     barbaric as she is said to be     to place herself disinterestedly at the head of an alliance ha ing for its object the maintenance of the balance of power of Europe, and it would sa e the world!"

"But how are you to get that balance?" Pierre was beginning. At that moment Anna Pa lo na came up and, looking se erely at Pierre, asked the Italian how he stood Russian climate.

The Italian's face instantly changed and assumed an offensi ely affected, sugary expression, e idently habitual to him when con ersing with women.

"I am so enchanted by the brilliancy of the wit and culture of the society, more especially of the feminine society, in which I ha e had the honor of being recei ed, that I ha e not yet had time to think of the climate," said he.

Not letting the abbe and Pierre escape, Anna Pa lo na, the more con eniently to keep them under obser ation, brought them into the larger circle.

CHAPTER I 

Just then another  isitor entered the drawing room  Prince Andrew Bolkonski, the little princess' husband.

He was a  ery handsome young man, of medium height, with firm, clearcut features.

E erything about him, from his weary, bored expression to his quiet, measured step, offered a most striking contrast to his quiet, little wife.

It was e ident that he not only knew e eryone in the drawing room, but had found them to be so tiresome that it wearied him to look at or listen to them.

And among all these faces that he found so tedious, none seemed to bore him so much as that of his pretty wife.

He turned away from her with a grimace that distorted his handsome face, kissed Anna Pa lo na's hand, and screwing up his eyes scanned the whole company.

"You are off to the war, Prince?" said Anna Pa lo na.

"General Kutuzo ," said Bolkonski, speaking French and stressing the last syllable of the general's name like a Frenchman, "has been pleased to take me as an aide de camp...."

"And Lise, your wife?"

"She will go to the country."

"Are you not ashamed to depri e us of your charming wife?"

"Andre," said his wife, addressing her husband in the same coquettish manner in which she spoke to other men, "the  icomte has been telling us such a tale about Mademoiselle George and Buonaparte!"

Prince Andrew screwed up his eyes and turned away.

Pierre, who from the moment Prince Andrew entered the room had watched him with glad, affectionate eyes, now came up and took his arm.

Before he looked round Prince Andrew frowned again, expressing his annoyance with whoe er was touching his arm, but when he saw Pierre's beaming face he ga e him an unexpectedly kind and pleasant smile.

"There now!... So you, too, are in the great world?" said he to Pierre.

"I knew you would be here," replied Pierre. "I will come to supper with you. May I?" he added in a low  oice so as not to disturb the  icomte who was continuing his story.

"No, impossible!" said Prince Andrew, laughing and pressing Pierre's hand to show that there was no need to ask the question.

He wished to say something more, but at that moment Prince  asili and his daughter got up to go and the two young men rose to let them pass.

"You must excuse me, dear  icomte," said Prince  asili to the Frenchman, holding him down by the slee e in a friendly way to pre ent his rising. "This unfortunate fete at the ambassador's depri es me of a pleasure, and obliges me to interrupt you.

I am  ery sorry to lea e your enchanting party," said he, turning to Anna Pa lo na.

His daughter, Princess Helene, passed between the chairs, lightly holding up the folds of her dress, and the smile shone still more radiantly on her beautiful face.

Pierre gazed at her with rapturous, almost frightened, eyes as she passed him.

" ery lo ely," said Prince Andrew.

" ery," said Pierre.

In passing Prince  asili seized Pierre's hand and said to Anna Pa lo na 

"Educate this bear for me! He has been staying with me a whole month and this is the first time I ha e seen him in society.

Nothing is so necessary for a young man as the society of cle er women."

Anna Pa lo na smiled and promised to take Pierre in hand. She knew his father to be a connection of Prince  asili's.

The elderly lady who had been sitting with the old aunt rose hurriedly and o ertook Prince  asili in the anteroom.

All the affectation of interest she had assumed had left her kindly and tear worn face and it now expressed only anxiety and fear.

"How about my son Boris, Prince?" said she, hurrying after him into the anteroom. "I can't remain any longer in Petersburg.

Tell me what news I may take back to my poor boy."

Although Prince  asili listened reluctantly and not  ery politely to the elderly lady, e en betraying some impatience, she ga e him an ingratiating and appealing smile, and took his hand that he might not go away.

"What would it cost you to say a word to the Emperor, and then he would be transferred to the Guards at once?" said she.

"Belie e me, Princess, I am ready to do all I can," answered Prince  asili, "but it is difficult for me to ask the Emperor. I should ad ise you to appeal to Rumyantse  through Prince Golitsyn. That would be the best way."

The elderly lady was a Princess Drubetskaya, belonging to one of the best families in Russia, but she was poor, and ha ing long been out of society had lost her former influential connections.

She had now come to Petersburg to procure an appointment in the Guards for her only son.

It was, in fact, solely to meet Prince  asili that she had obtained an in itation to Anna Pa lo na's reception and had sat listening to the  icomte's story.

Prince  asili's words frightened her, an embittered look clouded her once handsome face, but only for a moment  then she smiled again and clutched Prince  asili's arm more tightly.

"Listen to me, Prince," said she. "I ha e ne er yet asked you for anything and I ne er will again, nor ha e I e er reminded you of my father's friendship for you  but now I entreat you for God's sake to do this for my son     and I shall always regard you as a benefactor," she added hurriedly. "No, don't be angry, but promise!

I ha e asked Golitsyn and he has refused.

Be the kindhearted man you always were," she said, trying to smile though tears were in her eyes.

"Papa, we shall be late," said Princess Helene, turning her beautiful head and looking o er her classically molded shoulder as she stood waiting by the door.

Influence in society, howe er, is a capital which has to be economized if it is to last.

Prince  asili knew this, and ha ing once realized that if he asked on behalf of all who begged of him, he would soon be unable to ask for himself, he became chary of using his influence.

But in Princess Drubetskaya's case he felt, after her second appeal, something like qualms of conscience.

She had reminded him of what was quite true  he had been indebted to her father for the first steps in his career.

Moreo er, he could see by her manners that she was one of those women     mostly mothers     who, ha ing once made up their minds, will not rest until they ha e gained their end, and are prepared if necessary to go on insisting day after day and hour after hour, and e en to make scenes.

This last consideration mo ed him.

"My dear Anna Mikhaylo na," said he with his usual familiarity and weariness of tone, "it is almost impossible for me to do what you ask  but to pro e my de otion to you and how I respect your father's memory, I will do the impossible     your son shall be transferred to the Guards. Here is my hand on it.

Are you satisfied?"

"My dear benefactor!

This is what I expected from you     I knew your kindness!"

He turned to go.

"Wait     just a word!

When he has been transferred to the Guards..." she faltered. "You are on good terms with Michael Ilariono ich Kutuzo ... recommend Boris to him as adjutant!

Then I shall be at rest, and then..."

Prince  asili smiled.

"No, I won't promise that.

You don't know how Kutuzo  is pestered since his appointment as Commander in Chief.

He told me himself that all the Moscow ladies ha e conspired to gi e him all their sons as adjutants."

"No, but do promise! I won't let you go! My dear benefactor..."

"Papa," said his beautiful daughter in the same tone as before, "we shall be late."

"Well, au re oir! Good by!

You hear her?"

"Then tomorrow you will speak to the Emperor?"

"Certainly  but about Kutuzo , I don't promise."

"Do promise, do promise,  asili!" cried Anna Mikhaylo na as he went, with the smile of a coquettish girl, which at one time probably came naturally to her, but was now  ery ill suited to her careworn face.

Apparently she had forgotten her age and by force of habit employed all the old feminine arts.

But as soon as the prince had gone her face resumed its former cold, artificial expression.

She returned to the group where the  icomte was still talking, and again pretended to listen, while waiting till it would be time to lea e.

Her task was accomplished.

CHAPTER  

"And what do you think of this latest comedy, the coronation at Milan?" asked Anna Pa lo na, "and of the comedy of the people of Genoa and Lucca laying their petitions before Monsieur Buonaparte, and Monsieur Buonaparte sitting on a throne and granting the petitions of the nations?

Adorable!

It is enough to make one's head whirl!

It is as if the whole world had gone crazy."

Prince Andrew looked Anna Pa lo na straight in the face with a sarcastic smile.

"'Dieu me la donne, gare a qui la touche!'   They say he was  ery fine when he said that," he remarked, repeating the words in Italian  "'Dio mi l'ha dato.

Guai a chi la tocchi!'"   God has gi en it to me, let him who touches it beware!

"I hope this will pro e the last drop that will make the glass run o er," Anna Pa lo na continued.

"The so ereigns will not be able to endure this man who is a menace to e erything."

"The so ereigns?

I do not speak of Russia," said the  icomte, polite but hopeless  "The so ereigns, madame...

What ha e they done for Louis X II, for the Queen, or for Madame Elizabeth?

Nothing!" and he became more animated. "And belie e me, they are reaping the reward of their betrayal of the Bourbon cause.

The so ereigns!

Why, they are sending ambassadors to compliment the usurper."

And sighing disdainfully, he again changed his position.

Prince Hippolyte, who had been gazing at the  icomte for some time through his lorgnette, suddenly turned completely round toward the little princess, and ha ing asked for a needle began tracing the Conde coat of arms on the table.

He explained this to her with as much gra ity as if she had asked him to do it.

"Baton de gueules, engrele de gueules d'azur     maison Conde," said he.

The princess listened, smiling.

"If Buonaparte remains on the throne of France a year longer," the  icomte continued, with the air of a man who, in a matter with which he is better acquainted than anyone else, does not listen to others but follows the current of his own thoughts, "things will ha e gone too far.

By intrigues,  iolence, exile, and executions, French society     I mean good French society     will ha e been fore er destroyed, and then..."

He shrugged his shoulders and spread out his hands.

Pierre wished to make a remark, for the con ersation interested him, but Anna Pa lo na, who had him under obser ation, interrupted 

"The Emperor Alexander," said she, with the melancholy which always accompanied any reference of hers to the Imperial family, "has declared that he will lea e it to the French people themsel es to choose their own form of go ernment  and I belie e that once free from the usurper, the whole nation will certainly throw itself into the arms of its rightful king," she concluded, trying to be amiable to the royalist emigrant.

"That is doubtful," said Prince Andrew. "Monsieur le  icomte quite rightly supposes that matters ha e already gone too far.

I think it will be difficult to return to the old regime."

"From what I ha e heard," said Pierre, blushing and breaking into the con ersation, "almost all the aristocracy has already gone o er to Bonaparte's side."

"It is the Buonapartists who say that," replied the  icomte without looking at Pierre. "At the present time it is difficult to know the real state of French public opinion."

"Bonaparte has said so," remarked Prince Andrew with a sarcastic smile.

It was e ident that he did not like the  icomte and was aiming his remarks at him, though without looking at him.

"'I showed them the path to glory, but they did not follow it,'" Prince Andrew continued after a short silence, again quoting Napoleon's words. "'I opened my antechambers and they crowded in.'

I do not know how far he was justified in saying so." "Not in the least," replied the  icomte. "After the murder of the duc e en the most partial ceased to regard him as a hero.

If to some people," he went on, turning to Anna Pa lo na, "he e er was a hero, after the murder of the duc there was one martyr more in hea en and one hero less on earth."

Before Anna Pa lo na and the others had time to smile their appreciation of the  icomte's epigram, Pierre again broke into the con ersation, and though Anna Pa lo na felt sure he would say something inappropriate, she was unable to stop him.

"The execution of the Duc d'Enghien," declared Monsieur Pierre, "was a political necessity, and it seems to me that Napoleon showed greatness of soul by not fearing to take on himself the whole responsibility of that deed."

"Dieu! Mon Dieu!" muttered Anna Pa lo na in a terrified whisper.

"What, Monsieur Pierre...

Do you consider that assassination shows greatness of soul?" said the little princess, smiling and drawing her work nearer to her.

"Oh!

Oh!" exclaimed se eral  oices.

"Capital!" said Prince Hippolyte in English, and began slapping his knee with the palm of his hand.

The  icomte merely shrugged his shoulders.

Pierre looked solemnly at his audience o er his spectacles and continued.

"I say so," he continued desperately, "because the Bourbons fled from the Re olution lea ing the people to anarchy, and Napoleon alone understood the Re olution and quelled it, and so for the general good, he could not stop short for the sake of one man's life."

"Won't you come o er to the other table?" suggested Anna Pa lo na.

But Pierre continued his speech without heeding her.

"No," cried he, becoming more and more eager, "Napoleon is great because he rose superior to the Re olution, suppressed its abuses, preser ed all that was good in it     equality of citizenship and freedom of speech and of the press     and only for that reason did he obtain power."

"Yes, if ha ing obtained power, without a ailing himself of it to commit murder he had restored it to the rightful king, I should ha e called him a great man," remarked the  icomte.

"He could not do that.

The people only ga e him power that he might rid them of the Bourbons and because they saw that he was a great man.

The Re olution was a grand thing!" continued Monsieur Pierre, betraying by this desperate and pro ocati e proposition his extreme youth and his wish to express all that was in his mind.

"What? Re olution and regicide a grand thing?... Well, after that... But won't you come to this other table?" repeated Anna Pa lo na.

"Rousseau's Contrat social," said the  icomte with a tolerant smile.

"I am not speaking of regicide, I am speaking about ideas."

"Yes  ideas of robbery, murder, and regicide," again interjected an ironical  oice.

"Those were extremes, no doubt, but they are not what is most important. What is important are the rights of man, emancipation from prejudices, and equality of citizenship, and all these ideas Napoleon has retained in full force."

"Liberty and equality," said the  icomte contemptuously, as if at last deciding seriously to pro e to this youth how foolish his words were, "high sounding words which ha e long been discredited.

Who does not lo e liberty and equality?

E en our Sa iour preached liberty and equality.

Ha e people since the Re olution become happier?

On the contrary.

We wanted liberty, but Buonaparte has destroyed it."

Prince Andrew kept looking with an amused smile from Pierre to the  icomte and from the  icomte to their hostess.

In the first moment of Pierre's outburst Anna Pa lo na, despite her social experience, was horror struck. But when she saw that Pierre's sacrilegious words had not exasperated the  icomte, and had con inced herself that it was impossible to stop him, she rallied her forces and joined the  icomte in a  igorous attack on the orator.

"But, my dear Monsieur Pierre," said she, "how do you explain the fact of a great man executing a duc     or e en an ordinary man who     is innocent and untried?"

"I should like," said the  icomte, "to ask how monsieur explains the 18th Brumaire  was not that an imposture?

It was a swindle, and not at all like the conduct of a great man!"

"And the prisoners he killed in Africa? That was horrible!" said the little princess, shrugging her shoulders.

"He's a low fellow, say what you will," remarked Prince Hippolyte.

Pierre, not knowing whom to answer, looked at them all and smiled.

His smile was unlike the half smile of other people.

When he smiled, his gra e, e en rather gloomy, look was instantaneously replaced by another     a childlike, kindly, e en rather silly look, which seemed to ask forgi eness.

The  icomte who was meeting him for the first time saw clearly that this young Jacobin was not so terrible as his words suggested.

All were silent.

"How do you expect him to answer you all at once?" said Prince Andrew. "Besides, in the actions of a statesman one has to distinguish between his acts as a pri ate person, as a general, and as an emperor.

So it seems to me."

"Yes, yes, of course!" Pierre chimed in, pleased at the arri al of this reinforcement.

"One must admit," continued Prince Andrew, "that Napoleon as a man was great on the bridge of Arcola, and in the hospital at Jaffa where he ga e his hand to the plague stricken  but... but there are other acts which it is difficult to justify."

Prince Andrew, who had e idently wished to tone down the awkwardness of Pierre's remarks, rose and made a sign to his wife that it was time to go.

Suddenly Prince Hippolyte started up making signs to e eryone to attend, and asking them all to be seated began 

"I was told a charming Moscow story today and must treat you to it.

Excuse me,  icomte     I must tell it in Russian or the point will be lost...."

And Prince Hippolyte began to tell his story in such Russian as a Frenchman would speak after spending about a year in Russia.

E eryone waited, so emphatically and eagerly did he demand their attention to his story.

"There is in Moscow a lady, une dame, and she is  ery stingy.

She must ha e two footmen behind her carriage, and  ery big ones.

That was her taste.

And she had a lady's maid, also big.

She said..."

Here Prince Hippolyte paused, e idently collecting his ideas with difficulty.

"She said... Oh yes! She said, 'Girl,' to the maid, 'put on a li ery, get up behind the carriage, and come with me while I make some calls.'"

Here Prince Hippolyte spluttered and burst out laughing long before his audience, which produced an effect unfa orable to the narrator.

Se eral persons, among them the elderly lady and Anna Pa lo na, did howe er smile.

"She went.

Suddenly there was a great wind.

The girl lost her hat and her long hair came down...."

Here he could contain himself no longer and went on, between gasps of laughter 

"And the whole world knew...."

And so the anecdote ended.

Though it was unintelligible why he had told it, or why it had to be told in Russian, still Anna Pa lo na and the others appreciated Prince Hippolyte's social tact in so agreeably ending Pierre's unpleasant and unamiable outburst.

After the anecdote the con ersation broke up into insignificant small talk about the last and next balls, about theatricals, and who would meet whom, and when and where.

CHAPTER  I

Ha ing thanked Anna Pa lo na for her charming soiree, the guests began to take their lea e.

Pierre was ungainly.

Stout, about the a erage height, broad, with huge red hands  he did not know, as the saying is, how to enter a drawing room and still less how to lea e one  that is, how to say something particularly agreeable before going away.

Besides this he was absent minded.

When he rose to go, he took up instead of his own, the general's three cornered hat, and held it, pulling at the plume, till the general asked him to restore it.

All his absent mindedness and inability to enter a room and con erse in it was, howe er, redeemed by his kindly, simple, and modest expression.

Anna Pa lo na turned toward him and, with a Christian mildness that expressed forgi eness of his indiscretion, nodded and said 

"I hope to see you again, but I also hope you will change your opinions, my dear Monsieur Pierre."

When she said this, he did not reply and only bowed, but again e erybody saw his smile, which said nothing, unless perhaps,

"Opinions are opinions, but you see what a capital, good natured fellow I am."

And e eryone, including Anna Pa lo na, felt this.

Prince Andrew had gone out into the hall, and, turning his shoulders to the footman who was helping him on with his cloak, listened indifferently to his wife's chatter with Prince Hippolyte who had also come into the hall.

Prince Hippolyte stood close to the pretty, pregnant princess, and stared fixedly at her through his eyeglass.

"Go in, Annette, or you will catch cold," said the little princess, taking lea e of Anna Pa lo na. "It is settled," she added in a low  oice.

Anna Pa lo na had already managed to speak to Lise about the match she contemplated between Anatole and the little princess' sister in law.

"I rely on you, my dear," said Anna Pa lo na, also in a low tone. "Write to her and let me know how her father looks at the matter.

Au re oir!"     and she left the hall.

Prince Hippolyte approached the little princess and, bending his face close to her, began to whisper something.

Two footmen, the princess' and his own, stood holding a shawl and a cloak, waiting for the con ersation to finish. They listened to the French sentences which to them were meaningless, with an air of understanding but not wishing to appear to do so.

The princess as usual spoke smilingly and listened with a laugh.

Either from awkwardness or intentionally (no one could ha e said which) after the shawl had been adjusted he kept his arm around her for a long time, as though embracing her.

Still smiling, she gracefully mo ed away, turning and glancing at her husband.

Prince Andrew's eyes were closed, so weary and sleepy did he seem.

"Are you ready?" he asked his wife, looking past her.

Prince Hippolyte hurriedly put on his cloak, which in the latest fashion reached to his  ery heels, and, stumbling in it, ran out into the porch following the princess, whom a footman was helping into the carriage.

"Princesse, au re oir," cried he, stumbling with his tongue as well as with his feet.

The princess, picking up her dress, was taking her seat in the dark carriage, her husband was adjusting his saber  Prince Hippolyte, under pretense of helping, was in e eryone's way.

"I am expecting you, Pierre," said the same  oice, but gently and affectionately.

The postilion started, the carriage wheels rattled.
