You know the story of the handkerchief?

It is delightful!"

And the talkati e Dolgoruko , turning now to Boris, now to Prince Andrew, told how Bonaparte wishing to test Marko , our ambassador, purposely dropped a handkerchief in front of him and stood looking at Marko , probably expecting Marko  to pick it up for him, and how Marko  immediately dropped his own beside it and picked it up without touching Bonaparte's.

"Delightful!" said Bolkonski. "But I ha e come to you, Prince, as a petitioner on behalf of this young man.

You see..." but before Prince Andrew could finish, an aide de camp came in to summon Dolgoruko  to the Emperor.

"Oh, what a nuisance," said Dolgoruko , getting up hurriedly and pressing the hands of Prince Andrew and Boris. "You know I should be  ery glad to do all in my power both for you and for this dear young man." Again he pressed the hand of the latter with an expression of good natured, sincere, and animated le ity. "But you see... another time!"

Boris was excited by the thought of being so close to the higher powers as he felt himself to be at that moment.

He was conscious that here he was in contact with the springs that set in motion the enormous mo ements of the mass of which in his regiment he felt himself a tiny, obedient, and insignificant atom.

They followed Prince Dolgoruko  out into the corridor and met     coming out of the door of the Emperor's room by which Dolgoruko  had entered     a short man in ci ilian clothes with a cle er face and sharply projecting jaw which, without spoiling his face, ga e him a peculiar  i acity and shiftiness of expression.

This short man nodded to Dolgoruko  as to an intimate friend and stared at Prince Andrew with cool intensity, walking straight toward him and e idently expecting him to bow or to step out of his way.

Prince Andrew did neither  a look of animosity appeared on his face and the other turned away and went down the side of the corridor.

"Who was that?" asked Boris.

"He is one of the most remarkable, but to me most unpleasant of men     the Minister of Foreign Affairs, Prince Adam Czartoryski....

It is such men as he who decide the fate of nations," added Bolkonski with a sigh he could not suppress, as they passed out of the palace.

Next day, the army began its campaign, and up to the  ery battle of Austerlitz, Boris was unable to see either Prince Andrew or Dolgoruko  again and remained for a while with the Ismaylo  regiment.

CHAPTER X

At dawn on the sixteenth of No ember, Deniso 's squadron, in which Nicholas Rosto  ser ed and which was in Prince Bagration's detachment, mo ed from the place where it had spent the night, ad ancing into action as arranged, and after going behind other columns for about two thirds of a mile was stopped on the highroad.

Rosto  saw the Cossacks and then the first and second squadrons of hussars and infantry battalions and artillery pass by and go forward and then Generals Bagration and Dolgoruko  ride past with their adjutants.

All the fear before action which he had experienced as pre iously, all the inner struggle to conquer that fear, all his dreams of distinguishing himself as a true hussar in this battle, had been wasted.

Their squadron remained in reser e and Nicholas Rosto  spent that day in a dull and wretched mood.

At nine in the morning, he heard firing in front and shouts of hurrah, and saw wounded being brought back (there were not many of them), and at last he saw how a whole detachment of French ca alry was brought in, con oyed by a sotnya of Cossacks.

E idently the affair was o er and, though not big, had been a successful engagement.

The men and officers returning spoke of a brilliant  ictory, of the occupation of the town of Wischau and the capture of a whole French squadron.

The day was bright and sunny after a sharp night frost, and the cheerful glitter of that autumn day was in keeping with the news of  ictory which was con eyed, not only by the tales of those who had taken part in it, but also by the joyful expression on the faces of soldiers, officers, generals, and adjutants, as they passed Rosto  going or coming.

And Nicholas, who had  ainly suffered all the dread that precedes a battle and had spent that happy day in inacti ity, was all the more depressed.

"Come here, Wosto . Let's dwink to dwown our gwief!" shouted Deniso , who had settled down by the roadside with a flask and some food.

The officers gathered round Deniso 's canteen, eating and talking. "There!

They are bringing another!" cried one of the officers, indicating a capti e French dragoon who was being brought in on foot by two Cossacks.

One of them was leading by the bridle a fine large French horse he had taken from the prisoner.

"Sell us that horse!" Deniso  called out to the Cossacks.

"If you like, your honor!"

The officers got up and stood round the Cossacks and their prisoner.

The French dragoon was a young Alsatian who spoke French with a German accent.

He was breathless with agitation, his face was red, and when he heard some French spoken he at once began speaking to the officers, addressing first one, then another.

He said he would not ha e been taken, it was not his fault but the corporal's who had sent him to seize some horsecloths, though he had told him the Russians were there.

And at e ery word he added  "But don't hurt my little horse!" and stroked the animal.

It was plain that he did not quite grasp where he was.

Now he excused himself for ha ing been taken prisoner and now, imagining himself before his own officers, insisted on his soldierly discipline and zeal in the ser ice.

He brought with him into our rearguard all the freshness of atmosphere of the French army, which was so alien to us.

The Cossacks sold the horse for two gold pieces, and Rosto , being the richest of the officers now that he had recei ed his money, bought it.

"But don't hurt my little horse!" said the Alsatian good naturedly to Rosto  when the animal was handed o er to the hussar.

Rosto  smilingly reassured the dragoon and ga e him money.

"Alley!

Alley!" said the Cossack, touching the prisoner's arm to make him go on.

"The Emperor!

The Emperor!" was suddenly heard among the hussars.

All began to run and bustle, and Rosto  saw coming up the road behind him se eral riders with white plumes in their hats.

In a moment e eryone was in his place, waiting.

Rosto  did not know or remember how he ran to his place and mounted.

Instantly his regret at not ha ing been in action and his dejected mood amid people of whom he was weary had gone, instantly e ery thought of himself had  anished. He was filled with happiness at his nearness to the Emperor.

He felt that this nearness by itself made up to him for the day he had lost.

He was happy as a lo er when the longed for moment of meeting arri es.

Not daring to look round and without looking round, he was ecstatically conscious of his approach.

He felt it not only from the sound of the hoofs of the approaching ca alcade, but because as he drew near e erything grew brighter, more joyful, more significant, and more festi e around him.

Nearer and nearer to Rosto  came that sun shedding beams of mild and majestic light around, and already he felt himself en eloped in those beams, he heard his  oice, that kindly, calm, and majestic  oice that was yet so simple!

And as if in accord with Rosto 's feeling, there was a deathly stillness amid which was heard the Emperor's  oice.

"The Pa lograd hussars?" he inquired.

"The reser es, sire!" replied a  oice, a  ery human one compared to that which had said  "The Pa lograd hussars?"

The Emperor drew le el with Rosto  and halted.

Alexander's face was e en more beautiful than it had been three days before at the re iew.

It shone with such gaiety and youth, such innocent youth, that it suggested the li eliness of a fourteen year old boy, and yet it was the face of the majestic Emperor.

Casually, while sur eying the squadron, the Emperor's eyes met Rosto 's and rested on them for not more than two seconds.

Whether or no the Emperor understood what was going on in Rosto 's soul (it seemed to Rosto  that he understood e erything), at any rate his light blue eyes gazed for about two seconds into Rosto 's face. A gentle, mild light poured from them. Then all at once he raised his eyebrows, abruptly touched his horse with his left foot, and galloped on.

The younger Emperor could not restrain his wish to be present at the battle and, in spite of the remonstrances of his courtiers, at twel e o'clock left the third column with which he had been and galloped toward the  anguard.

Before he came up with the hussars, se eral adjutants met him with news of the successful result of the action.

This battle, which consisted in the capture of a French squadron, was represented as a brilliant  ictory o er the French, and so the Emperor and the whole army, especially while the smoke hung o er the battlefield, belie ed that the French had been defeated and were retreating against their will.

A few minutes after the Emperor had passed, the Pa lograd di ision was ordered to ad ance.

In Wischau itself, a petty German town, Rosto  saw the Emperor again.

In the market place, where there had been some rather hea y firing before the Emperor's arri al, lay se eral killed and wounded soldiers whom there had not been time to mo e.

The Emperor, surrounded by his suite of officers and courtiers, was riding a bobtailed chestnut mare, a different one from that which he had ridden at the re iew, and bending to one side he gracefully held a gold lorgnette to his eyes and looked at a soldier who lay prone, with blood on his unco ered head.

The wounded soldier was so dirty, coarse, and re olting that his proximity to the Emperor shocked Rosto .

Rosto  saw how the Emperor's rather round shoulders shuddered as if a cold shi er had run down them, how his left foot began con ulsi ely tapping the horse's side with the spur, and how the well trained horse looked round unconcerned and did not stir.

An adjutant, dismounting, lifted the soldier under the arms to place him on a stretcher that had been brought.

The soldier groaned.

"Gently, gently! Can't you do it more gently?" said the Emperor apparently suffering more than the dying soldier, and he rode away.

Rosto  saw tears filling the Emperor's eyes and heard him, as he was riding away, say to Czartoryski 

"What a terrible thing war is  what a terrible thing!

Quelle terrible chose que la guerre!"

The troops of the  anguard were stationed before Wischau, within sight of the enemy's lines, which all day long had yielded ground to us at the least firing.

The Emperor's gratitude was announced to the  anguard, rewards were promised, and the men recei ed a double ration of  odka.

The campfires crackled and the soldiers' songs resounded e en more merrily than on the pre ious night.

Deniso  celebrated his promotion to the rank of major, and Rosto , who had already drunk enough, at the end of the feast proposed the Emperor's health. "Not 'our So ereign, the Emperor,' as they say at official dinners," said he, "but the health of our So ereign, that good, enchanting, and great man! Let us drink to his health and to the certain defeat of the French!"

"If we fought before," he said, "not letting the French pass, as at Schon Grabern, what shall we not do now when he is at the front?

We will all die for him gladly!

Is it not so, gentlemen?

Perhaps I am not saying it right, I ha e drunk a good deal     but that is how I feel, and so do you too!

To the health of Alexander the First!

Hurrah!"

"Hurrah!" rang the enthusiastic  oices of the officers.

And the old ca alry captain, Kirsten, shouted enthusiastically and no less sincerely than the twenty year old Rosto .

When the officers had emptied and smashed their glasses, Kirsten filled others and, in shirt slee es and breeches, went glass in hand to the soldiers' bonfires and with his long gray mustache, his white chest showing under his open shirt, he stood in a majestic pose in the light of the campfire, wa ing his uplifted arm.

"Lads! here's to our So ereign, the Emperor, and  ictory o er our enemies! Hurrah!" he exclaimed in his dashing, old, hussar's baritone.

The hussars crowded round and responded heartily with loud shouts.

Late that night, when all had separated, Deniso  with his short hand patted his fa orite, Rosto , on the shoulder.

"As there's no one to fall in lo e with on campaign, he's fallen in lo e with the Tsar," he said.

"Deniso , don't make fun of it!" cried Rosto . "It is such a lofty, beautiful feeling, such a..."

"I belie e it, I belie e it, fwiend, and I share and appwo e..."

"No, you don't understand!"

And Rosto  got up and went wandering among the campfires, dreaming of what happiness it would be to die     not in sa ing the Emperor's life (he did not e en dare to dream of that), but simply to die before his eyes.

He really was in lo e with the Tsar and the glory of the Russian arms and the hope of future triumph.

And he was not the only man to experience that feeling during those memorable days preceding the battle of Austerlitz  nine tenths of the men in the Russian army were then in lo e, though less ecstatically, with their Tsar and the glory of the Russian arms.

CHAPTER XI

The next day the Emperor stopped at Wischau, and  illier, his physician, was repeatedly summoned to see him.

At headquarters and among the troops near by the news spread that the Emperor was unwell.

He ate nothing and had slept badly that night, those around him reported.

The cause of this indisposition was the strong impression made on his sensiti e mind by the sight of the killed and wounded.

At daybreak on the se enteenth, a French officer who had come with a flag of truce, demanding an audience with the Russian Emperor, was brought into Wischau from our outposts.

This officer was Sa ary.

The Emperor had only just fallen asleep and so Sa ary had to wait.

At midday he was admitted to the Emperor, and an hour later he rode off with Prince Dolgoruko  to the ad anced post of the French army.

It was rumored that Sa ary had been sent to propose to Alexander a meeting with Napoleon.

To the joy and pride of the whole army, a personal inter iew was refused, and instead of the So ereign, Prince Dolgoruko , the  ictor at Wischau, was sent with Sa ary to negotiate with Napoleon if, contrary to expectations, these negotiations were actuated by a real desire for peace.

Toward e ening Dolgoruko  came back, went straight to the Tsar, and remained alone with him for a long time.

On the eighteenth and nineteenth of No ember, the army ad anced two days' march and the enemy's outposts after a brief interchange of shots retreated.

In the highest army circles from midday on the nineteenth, a great, excitedly bustling acti ity began which lasted till the morning of the twentieth, when the memorable battle of Austerlitz was fought.

Till midday on the nineteenth, the acti ity     the eager talk, running to and fro, and dispatching of adjutants     was confined to the Emperor's headquarters. But on the afternoon of that day, this acti ity reached Kutuzo 's headquarters and the staffs of the commanders of columns.

By e ening, the adjutants had spread it to all ends and parts of the army, and in the night from the nineteenth to the twentieth, the whole eighty thousand allied troops rose from their bi ouacs to the hum of  oices, and the army swayed and started in one enormous mass six miles long.

The concentrated acti ity which had begun at the Emperor's headquarters in the morning and had started the whole mo ement that followed was like the first mo ement of the main wheel of a large tower clock.

One wheel slowly mo ed, another was set in motion, and a third, and wheels began to re ol e faster and faster, le ers and cogwheels to work, chimes to play, figures to pop out, and the hands to ad ance with regular motion as a result of all that acti ity.

Just as in the mechanism of a clock, so in the mechanism of the military machine, an impulse once gi en leads to the final result  and just as indifferently quiescent till the moment when motion is transmitted to them are the parts of the mechanism which the impulse has not yet reached.

Wheels creak on their axles as the cogs engage one another and the re ol ing pulleys whirr with the rapidity of their mo ement, but a neighboring wheel is as quiet and motionless as though it were prepared to remain so for a hundred years  but the moment comes when the le er catches it and obeying the impulse that wheel begins to creak and joins in the common motion the result and aim of which are beyond its ken.

Just as in a clock, the result of the complicated motion of innumerable wheels and pulleys is merely a slow and regular mo ement of the hands which show the time, so the result of all the complicated human acti ities of 160,000 Russians and French     all their passions, desires, remorse, humiliations, sufferings, outbursts of pride, fear, and enthusiasm     was only the loss of the battle of Austerlitz, the so called battle of the three Emperors     that is to say, a slow mo ement of the hand on the dial of human history.

Prince Andrew was on duty that day and in constant attendance on the commander in chief.

At six in the e ening, Kutuzo  went to the Emperor's headquarters and after staying but a short time with the Tsar went to see the grand marshal of the court, Count Tolstoy.

Bolkonski took the opportunity to go in to get some details of the coming action from Dolgoruko .

He felt that Kutuzo  was upset and dissatisfied about something and that at headquarters they were dissatisfied with him, and also that at the Emperor's headquarters e eryone adopted toward him the tone of men who know something others do not know  he therefore wished to speak to Dolgoruko .

"Well, how d'you do, my dear fellow?" said Dolgoruko , who was sitting at tea with Bilibin. "The fete is for tomorrow.

How is your old fellow? Out of sorts?"

"I won't say he is out of sorts, but I fancy he would like to be heard."

"But they heard him at the council of war and will hear him when he talks sense, but to temporize and wait for something now when Bonaparte fears nothing so much as a general battle is impossible."

"Yes, you ha e seen him?" said Prince Andrew. "Well, what is Bonaparte like?

How did he impress you?"

"Yes, I saw him, and am con inced that he fears nothing so much as a general engagement," repeated Dolgoruko , e idently prizing this general conclusion which he had arri ed at from his inter iew with Napoleon. "If he weren't afraid of a battle why did he ask for that inter iew? Why negotiate, and abo e all why retreat, when to retreat is so contrary to his method of conducting war?

Belie e me, he is afraid, afraid of a general battle. His hour has come!

Mark my words!"

"But tell me, what is he like, eh?" said Prince Andrew again.

"He is a man in a gray o ercoat,  ery anxious that I should call him 'Your Majesty,' but who, to his chagrin, got no title from me!

That's the sort of man he is, and nothing more," replied Dolgoruko , looking round at Bilibin with a smile.

"Despite my great respect for old Kutuzo ," he continued, "we should be a nice set of fellows if we were to wait about and so gi e him a chance to escape, or to trick us, now that we certainly ha e him in our hands!

No, we mustn't forget Su oro  and his rule     not to put yourself in a position to be attacked, but yourself to attack.

Belie e me in war the energy of young men often shows the way better than all the experience of old Cunctators."

"But in what position are we going to attack him?

I ha e been at the outposts today and it is impossible to say where his chief forces are situated," said Prince Andrew.

He wished to explain to Dolgoruko  a plan of attack he had himself formed.

"Oh, that is all the same," Dolgoruko  said quickly, and getting up he spread a map on the table. "All e entualities ha e been foreseen. If he is standing before Brunn..."

And Prince Dolgoruko  rapidly but indistinctly explained Weyrother's plan of a flanking mo ement.

Prince Andrew began to reply and to state his own plan, which might ha e been as good as Weyrother's, but for the disad antage that Weyrother's had already been appro ed.

As soon as Prince Andrew began to demonstrate the defects of the latter and the merits of his own plan, Prince Dolgoruko  ceased to listen to him and gazed absent mindedly not at the map, but at Prince Andrew's face.

"There will be a council of war at Kutuzo 's tonight, though  you can say all this there," remarked Dolgoruko .

"I will do so," said Prince Andrew, mo ing away from the map.

"Whate er are you bothering about, gentlemen?" said Bilibin, who, till then, had listened with an amused smile to their con ersation and now was e idently ready with a joke. "Whether tomorrow brings  ictory or defeat, the glory of our Russian arms is secure.

Except your Kutuzo , there is not a single Russian in command of a column!

The commanders are  Herr General Wimpfen, le Comte de Langeron, le Prince de Lichtenstein, le Prince, de Hohenlohe, and finally Prishprish, and so on like all those Polish names."

"Be quiet, backbiter!" said Dolgoruko . "It is not true  there are now two Russians, Milorado ich, and Dokhturo , and there would be a third, Count Arakchee , if his ner es were not too weak."

"Howe er, I think General Kutuzo  has come out," said Prince Andrew. "I wish you good luck and success, gentlemen!" he added and went out after shaking hands with Dolgoruko  and Bilibin.

On the way home, Prince Andrew could not refrain from asking Kutuzo , who was sitting silently beside him, what he thought of tomorrow's battle.

Kutuzo  looked sternly at his adjutant and, after a pause, replied 

"I think the battle will be lost, and so I told Count Tolstoy and asked him to tell the Emperor.

What do you think he replied?

'But, my dear general, I am engaged with rice and cutlets, look after military matters yourself!' Yes... That was the answer I got!"

CHAPTER XII

Shortly after nine o'clock that e ening, Weyrother dro e with his plans to Kutuzo 's quarters where the council of war was to be held.

All the commanders of columns were summoned to the commander in chief's and with the exception of Prince Bagration, who declined to come, were all there at the appointed time.

Weyrother, who was in full control of the proposed battle, by his eagerness and briskness presented a marked contrast to the dissatisfied and drowsy Kutuzo , who reluctantly played the part of chairman and president of the council of war.

Weyrother e idently felt himself to be at the head of a mo ement that had already become unrestrainable.

He was like a horse running downhill harnessed to a hea y cart.

Whether he was pulling it or being pushed by it he did not know, but rushed along at headlong speed with no time to consider what this mo ement might lead to.

Weyrother had been twice that e ening to the enemy's picket line to reconnoiter personally, and twice to the Emperors, Russian and Austrian, to report and explain, and to his headquarters where he had dictated the dispositions in German, and now, much exhausted, he arri ed at Kutuzo 's.

He was e idently so busy that he e en forgot to be polite to the commander in chief. He interrupted him, talked rapidly and indistinctly, without looking at the man he was addressing, and did not reply to questions put to him. He was bespattered with mud and had a pitiful, weary, and distracted air, though at the same time he was haughty and self confident.

Kutuzo  was occupying a nobleman's castle of modest dimensions near Ostralitz.

In the large drawing room which had become the commander in chief's office were gathered Kutuzo  himself, Weyrother, and the members of the council of war.

They were drinking tea, and only awaited Prince Bagration to begin the council.

At last Bagration's orderly came with the news that the prince could not attend.

Prince Andrew came in to inform the commander in chief of this and, a ailing himself of permission pre iously gi en him by Kutuzo  to be present at the council, he remained in the room.

"Since Prince Bagration is not coming, we may begin," said Weyrother, hurriedly rising from his seat and going up to the table on which an enormous map of the en irons of Brunn was spread out.

Kutuzo , with his uniform unbuttoned so that his fat neck bulged o er his collar as if escaping, was sitting almost asleep in a low chair, with his podgy old hands resting symmetrically on its arms.

At the sound of Weyrother's  oice, he opened his one eye with an effort.

"Yes, yes, if you please! It is already late," said he, and nodding his head he let it droop and again closed his eye.

If at first the members of the council thought that Kutuzo  was pretending to sleep, the sounds his nose emitted during the reading that followed pro ed that the commander in chief at that moment was absorbed by a far more serious matter than a desire to show his contempt for the dispositions or anything else     he was engaged in satisfying the irresistible human need for sleep.

He really was asleep.

Weyrother, with the gesture of a man too busy to lose a moment, glanced at Kutuzo  and, ha ing con inced himself that he was asleep, took up a paper and in a loud, monotonous  oice began to read out the dispositions for the impending battle, under a heading which he also read out 

"Dispositions for an attack on the enemy position behind Kobelnitz and Sokolnitz, No ember 30, 1805."

The dispositions were  ery complicated and difficult.

They began as follows 

"As the enemy's left wing rests on wooded hills and his right extends along Kobelnitz and Sokolnitz behind the ponds that are there, while we, on the other hand, with our left wing by far outflank his right, it is ad antageous to attack the enemy's latter wing especially if we occupy the  illages of Sokolnitz and Kobelnitz, whereby we can both fall on his flank and pursue him o er the plain between Schlappanitz and the Thuerassa forest, a oiding the defiles of Schlappanitz and Bellowitz which co er the enemy's front.

For this object it is necessary that... The first column marches... The second column marches... The third column marches..." and so on, read Weyrother.

The generals seemed to listen reluctantly to the difficult dispositions.

The tall, fair haired General Buxhowden stood, leaning his back against the wall, his eyes fixed on a burning candle, and seemed not to listen or e en to wish to be thought to listen.

Exactly opposite Weyrother, with his glistening wide open eyes fixed upon him and his mustache twisted upwards, sat the ruddy Milorado ich in a military pose, his elbows turned outwards, his hands on his knees, and his shoulders raised.

He remained stubbornly silent, gazing at Weyrother's face, and only turned away his eyes when the Austrian chief of staff finished reading.

Then Milorado ich looked round significantly at the other generals.

But one could not tell from that significant look whether he agreed or disagreed and was satisfied or not with the arrangements.

Next to Weyrother sat Count Langeron who, with a subtle smile that ne er left his typically southern French face during the whole time of the reading, gazed at his delicate fingers which rapidly twirled by its corners a gold snuffbox on which was a portrait.

In the middle of one of the longest sentences, he stopped the rotary motion of the snuffbox, raised his head, and with inimical politeness lurking in the corners of his thin lips interrupted Weyrother, wishing to say something. But the Austrian general, continuing to read, frowned angrily and jerked his elbows, as if to say  "You can tell me your  iews later, but now be so good as to look at the map and listen."

Langeron lifted his eyes with an expression of perplexity, turned round to Milorado ich as if seeking an explanation, but meeting the latter's impressi e but meaningless gaze drooped his eyes sadly and again took to twirling his snuffbox.

"A geography lesson!" he muttered as if to himself, but loud enough to be heard.

Przebyszewski, with respectful but dignified politeness, held his hand to his ear toward Weyrother, with the air of a man absorbed in attention.

Dohkturo , a little man, sat opposite Weyrother, with an assiduous and modest mien, and stooping o er the outspread map conscientiously studied the dispositions and the unfamiliar locality.

He asked Weyrother se eral times to repeat words he had not clearly heard and the difficult names of  illages.

Weyrother complied and Dohkturo  noted them down.

When the reading which lasted more than an hour was o er, Langeron again brought his snuffbox to rest and, without looking at Weyrother or at anyone in particular, began to say how difficult it was to carry out such a plan in which the enemy's position was assumed to be known, whereas it was perhaps not known, since the enemy was in mo ement.

Langeron's objections were  alid but it was ob ious that their chief aim was to show General Weyrother     who had read his dispositions with as much self confidence as if he were addressing school children     that he had to do, not with fools, but with men who could teach him something in military matters.

When the monotonous sound of Weyrother's  oice ceased, Kutuzo  opened his eye as a miller wakes up when the soporific drone of the mill wheel is interrupted.     He listened to what Langeron said, as if remarking, "So you are still at that silly business!" quickly closed his eye again, and let his head sink still lower.

Langeron, trying as  irulently as possible to sting Weyrother's  anity as author of the military plan, argued that Bonaparte might easily attack instead of being attacked, and so render the whole of this plan perfectly worthless.

Weyrother met all objections with a firm and contemptuous smile, e idently prepared beforehand to meet all objections be they what they might.

"If he could attack us, he would ha e done so today," said he.

"So you think he is powerless?" said Langeron.

"He has forty thousand men at most," replied Weyrother, with the smile of a doctor to whom an old wife wishes to explain the treatment of a case.

"In that case he is in iting his doom by awaiting our attack," said Langeron, with a subtly ironical smile, again glancing round for support to Milorado ich who was near him.

But Milorado ich was at that moment e idently thinking of anything rather than of what the generals were disputing about.

"Ma foi!" said he, "tomorrow we shall see all that on the battlefield."

Weyrother again ga e that smile which seemed to say that to him it was strange and ridiculous to meet objections from Russian generals and to ha e to pro e to them what he had not merely con inced himself of, but had also con inced the so ereign Emperors of.

"The enemy has quenched his fires and a continual noise is heard from his camp," said he. "What does that mean? Either he is retreating, which is the only thing we need fear, or he is changing his position." (He smiled ironically.)

"But e en if he also took up a position in the Thuerassa, he merely sa es us a great deal of trouble and all our arrangements to the minutest detail remain the same."

"How is that?..." began Prince Andrew, who had for long been waiting an opportunity to express his doubts.

Kutuzo  here woke up, coughed hea ily, and looked round at the generals.

"Gentlemen, the dispositions for tomorrow     or rather for today, for it is past midnight     cannot now be altered," said he. "You ha e heard them, and we shall all do our duty.

But before a battle, there is nothing more important..." he paused, "than to ha e a good sleep."

He mo ed as if to rise.

The generals bowed and retired.

It was past midnight.

Prince Andrew went out.

The council of war, at which Prince Andrew had not been able to express his opinion as he had hoped to, left on him a  ague and uneasy impression.

Whether Dolgoruko  and Weyrother, or Kutuzo , Langeron, and the others who did not appro e of the plan of attack, were right     he did not know.

"But was it really not possible for Kutuzo  to state his  iews plainly to the Emperor?

Is it possible that on account of court and personal considerations tens of thousands of li es, and my life, my life," he thought, "must be risked?"

"Yes, it is  ery likely that I shall be killed tomorrow," he thought.

And suddenly, at this thought of death, a whole series of most distant, most intimate, memories rose in his imagination  he remembered his last parting from his father and his wife  he remembered the days when he first lo ed her.

He thought of her pregnancy and felt sorry for her and for himself, and in a ner ously emotional and softened mood he went out of the hut in which he was billeted with Nes itski and began to walk up and down before it.

The night was foggy and through the fog the moonlight gleamed mysteriously.

"Yes, tomorrow, tomorrow!" he thought. "Tomorrow e erything may be o er for me! All these memories will be no more, none of them will ha e any meaning for me.

Tomorrow perhaps, e en certainly, I ha e a presentiment that for the first time I shall ha e to show all I can do."

And his fancy pictured the battle, its loss, the concentration of fighting at one point, and the hesitation of all the commanders.

And then that happy moment, that Toulon for which he had so long waited, presents itself to him at last.

He firmly and clearly expresses his opinion to Kutuzo , to Weyrother, and to the Emperors.

All are struck by the justness of his  iews, but no one undertakes to carry them out, so he takes a regiment, a di ision stipulates that no one is to interfere with his arrangements     leads his di ision to the decisi e point, and gains the  ictory alone.

"But death and suffering?" suggested another  oice.

Prince Andrew, howe er, did not answer that  oice and went on dreaming of his triumphs.

The dispositions for the next battle are planned by him alone.

Nominally he is only an adjutant on Kutuzo 's staff, but he does e erything alone.

The next battle is won by him alone.

Kutuzo  is remo ed and he is appointed... "Well and then?" asked the other  oice. "If before that you are not ten times wounded, killed, or betrayed, well... what then?..."

"Well then," Prince Andrew answered himself, "I don't know what will happen and don't want to know, and can't, but if I want this     want glory, want to be known to men, want to be lo ed by them, it is not my fault that I want it and want nothing but that and li e only for that.

Yes, for that alone!

I shall ne er tell anyone, but, oh God! what am I to do if I lo e nothing but fame and men's esteem?

Death, wounds, the loss of family     I fear nothing.

And precious and dear as many persons are to me     father, sister, wife     those dearest to me     yet dreadful and unnatural as it seems, I would gi e them all at once for a moment of glory, of triumph o er men, of lo e from men I don't know and ne er shall know, for the lo e of these men here," he thought, as he listened to  oices in Kutuzo 's courtyard.

The  oices were those of the orderlies who were packing up  one  oice, probably a coachman's, was teasing Kutuzo 's old cook whom Prince Andrew knew, and who was called Tit. He was saying,

"Tit, I say, Tit!"

"Well?" returned the old man.

"Go, Tit, thresh a bit!" said the wag.

"Oh, go to the de il!" called out a  oice, drowned by the laughter of the orderlies and ser ants.

"All the same, I lo e and  alue nothing but triumph o er them all, I  alue this mystic power and glory that is floating here abo e me in this mist!"

CHAPTER XIII

That same night, Rosto  was with a platoon on skirmishing duty in front of Bagration's detachment.

His hussars were placed along the line in couples and he himself rode along the line trying to master the sleepiness that kept coming o er him.

An enormous space, with our army's campfires dimly glowing in the fog, could be seen behind him  in front of him was misty darkness.

Rosto  could see nothing, peer as he would into that foggy distance  now something gleamed gray, now there was something black, now little lights seemed to glimmer where the enemy ought to be, now he fancied it was only something in his own eyes.

His eyes kept closing, and in his fancy appeared     now the Emperor, now Deniso , and now Moscow memories     and he again hurriedly opened his eyes and saw close before him the head and ears of the horse he was riding, and sometimes, when he came within six paces of them, the black figures of hussars, but in the distance was still the same misty darkness.

"Why not?... It might easily happen," thought Rosto , "that the Emperor will meet me and gi e me an order as he would to any other officer  he'll say  'Go and find out what's there.'

There are many stories of his getting to know an officer in just such a chance way and attaching him to himself!

What if he ga e me a place near him?

Oh, how I would guard him, how I would tell him the truth, how I would unmask his decei ers!" And in order to realize  i idly his lo e de otion to the so ereign, Rosto  pictured to himself an enemy or a deceitful German, whom he would not only kill with pleasure but whom he would slap in the face before the Emperor.

Suddenly a distant shout aroused him.

He started and opened his eyes.

"Where am I?

Oh yes, in the skirmishing line... pass and watchword     shaft, Olmutz.

What a nuisance that our squadron will be in reser e tomorrow," he thought.

"I'll ask lea e to go to the front, this may be my only chance of seeing the Emperor.

It won't be long now before I am off duty.

I'll take another turn and when I get back I'll go to the general and ask him."

He readjusted himself in the saddle and touched up his horse to ride once more round his hussars.

It seemed to him that it was getting lighter.

To the left he saw a sloping descent lit up, and facing it a black knoll that seemed as steep as a wall.

On this knoll there was a white patch that Rosto  could not at all make out  was it a glade in the wood lit up by the moon, or some unmelted snow, or some white houses?

He e en thought something mo ed on that white spot.

"I expect it's snow... that spot... a spot     une tache," he thought.

"There now... it's not a tache...

Natasha... sister, black eyes...

Na... tasha... (Won't she be surprised when I tell her how I' e seen the Emperor?) Natasha... take my sabretache..."     "Keep to the right, your honor, there are bushes here," came the  oice of an hussar, past whom Rosto  was riding in the act of falling asleep.

Rosto  lifted his head that had sunk almost to his horse's mane and pulled up beside the hussar.

He was succumbing to irresistible, youthful, childish drowsiness.

"But what was I thinking? I mustn't forget.

How shall I speak to the Emperor?

No, that's not it     that's tomorrow.

Oh yes!

Natasha... sabretache... saber them... Whom?

The hussars...

Ah, the hussars with mustaches. Along the T erskaya Street rode the hussar with mustaches... I thought about him too, just opposite Gurye 's house... Old Gurye .... Oh, but Deniso 's a fine fellow.

But that's all nonsense.

The chief thing is that the Emperor is here.

How he looked at me and wished to say something, but dared not.... No, it was I who dared not.

But that's nonsense, the chief thing is not to forget the important thing I was thinking of.

Yes, Na tasha, sabretache, oh, yes, yes!

That's right!" And his head once more sank to his horse's neck.

All at once it seemed to him that he was being fired at.

"What?

What?

What?... Cut them down!

What?..." said Rosto , waking up.

At the moment he opened his eyes he heard in front of him, where the enemy was, the long drawn shouts of thousands of  oices.

His horse and the horse of the hussar near him pricked their ears at these shouts.

O er there, where the shouting came from, a fire flared up and went out again, then another, and all along the French line on the hill fires flared up and the shouting grew louder and louder.

Rosto  could hear the sound of French words but could not distinguish them.

The din of many  oices was too great  all he could hear was  "ahahah!" and "rrrr!"

"What's that?

What do you make of it?" said Rosto  to the hussar beside him. "That must be the enemy's camp!"

The hussar did not reply.

"Why, don't you hear it?" Rosto  asked again, after waiting for a reply.

"Who can tell, your honor?" replied the hussar reluctantly.

"From the direction, it must be the enemy," repeated Rosto .

"It may be he or it may be nothing," muttered the hussar. "It's dark...

Steady!" he cried to his fidgeting horse.

Rosto 's horse was also getting resti e  it pawed the frozen ground, pricking its ears at the noise and looking at the lights.

The shouting grew still louder and merged into a general roar that only an army of se eral thousand men could produce.

The lights spread farther and farther, probably along the line of the French camp.

Rosto  no longer wanted to sleep.

The gay triumphant shouting of the enemy army had a stimulating effect on him. " i e l'Empereur! L'Empereur!" he now heard distinctly.

"They can't be far off, probably just beyond the stream," he said to the hussar beside him.

The hussar only sighed without replying and coughed angrily.

The sound of horse's hoofs approaching at a trot along the line of hussars was heard, and out of the foggy darkness the figure of a sergeant of hussars suddenly appeared, looming huge as an elephant.

"Your honor, the generals!" said the sergeant, riding up to Rosto .

Rosto , still looking round toward the fires and the shouts, rode with the sergeant to meet some mounted men who were riding along the line.

One was on a white horse.

Prince Bagration and Prince Dolgoruko  with their adjutants had come to witness the curious phenomenon of the lights and shouts in the enemy's camp.

Rosto  rode up to Bagration, reported to him, and then joined the adjutants listening to what the generals were saying.

"Belie e me," said Prince Dolgoruko , addressing Bagration, "it is nothing but a trick! He has retreated and ordered the rearguard to kindle fires and make a noise to decei e us."

"Hardly," said Bagration. "I saw them this e ening on that knoll  if they had retreated they would ha e withdrawn from that too....

Officer!" said Bagration to Rosto , "are the enemy's skirmishers still there?"

"They were there this e ening, but now I don't know, your excellency.

Shall I go with some of my hussars to see?" replied Rosto .

Bagration stopped and, before replying, tried to see Rosto 's face in the mist.

"Well, go and see," he said, after a pause.

"Yes, sir."

Rosto  spurred his horse, called to Sergeant Fedchenko and two other hussars, told them to follow him, and trotted downhill in the direction from which the shouting came.

He felt both frightened and pleased to be riding alone with three hussars into that mysterious and dangerous misty distance where no one had been before him.

Bagration called to him from the hill not to go beyond the stream, but Rosto  pretended not to hear him and did not stop but rode on and on, continually mistaking bushes for trees and gullies for men and continually disco ering his mistakes.

Ha ing descended the hill at a trot, he no longer saw either our own or the enemy's fires, but heard the shouting of the French more loudly and distinctly.

In the  alley he saw before him something like a ri er, but when he reached it he found it was a road.

Ha ing come out onto the road he reined in his horse, hesitating whether to ride along it or cross it and ride o er the black field up the hillside.

To keep to the road which gleamed white in the mist would ha e been safer because it would be easier to see people coming along it.

"Follow me!" said he, crossed the road, and began riding up the hill at a gallop toward the point where the French pickets had been standing that e ening.

"Your honor, there he is!" cried one of the hussars behind him.

And before Rosto  had time to make out what the black thing was that had suddenly appeared in the fog, there was a flash, followed by a report, and a bullet whizzing high up in the mist with a plainti e sound passed out of hearing.

Another musket missed fire but flashed in the pan.

Rosto  turned his horse and galloped back.

Four more reports followed at inter als, and the bullets passed somewhere in the fog singing in different tones.

Rosto  reined in his horse, whose spirits had risen, like his own, at the firing, and went back at a footpace.

"Well, some more! Some more!" a merry  oice was saying in his soul.

But no more shots came.

Only when approaching Bagration did Rosto  let his horse gallop again, and with his hand at the salute rode up to the general.

Dolgoruko  was still insisting that the French had retreated and had only lit fires to decei e us.

"What does that pro e?" he was saying as Rosto  rode up. "They might retreat and lea e the pickets."

"It's plain that they ha e not all gone yet, Prince," said Bagration. "Wait till tomorrow morning, we'll find out e erything tomorrow."

"The picket is still on the hill, your excellency, just where it was in the e ening," reported Rosto , stooping forward with his hand at the salute and unable to repress the smile of delight induced by his ride and especially by the sound of the bullets.

" ery good,  ery good," said Bagration. "Thank you, officer."

"Your excellency," said Rosto , "may I ask a fa or?"

"What is it?"

"Tomorrow our squadron is to be in reser e. May I ask to be attached to the first squadron?"

"What's your name?"

"Count Rosto ."

"Oh,  ery well, you may stay in attendance on me."

"Count Ilya Rosto 's son?" asked Dolgoruko .

But Rosto  did not reply.

"Then I may reckon on it, your excellency?"

"I will gi e the order."

"Tomorrow  ery likely I may be sent with some message to the Emperor," thought Rosto . "Thank God!"

The fires and shouting in the enemy's army were occasioned by the fact that while Napoleon's proclamation was being read to the troops the Emperor himself rode round his bi ouacs.

The soldiers, on seeing him, lit wisps of straw and ran after him, shouting, " i e l'Empereur!"

Napoleon's proclamation was as follows 

Soldiers!

The Russian army is ad ancing against you to a enge the Austrian army of Ulm.

They are the same battalions you broke at Hollabrunn and ha e pursued e er since to this place.

The position we occupy is a strong one, and while they are marching to go round me on the right they will expose a flank to me.

Soldiers!

I will myself direct your battalions.

I will keep out of fire if you with your habitual  alor carry disorder and confusion into the enemy's ranks, but should  ictory be in doubt, e en for a moment, you will see your Emperor exposing himself to the first blows of the enemy, for there must be no doubt of  ictory, especially on this day when what is at stake is the honor of the French infantry, so necessary to the honor of our nation.

Do not break your ranks on the plea of remo ing the wounded!

Let e ery man be fully imbued with the thought that we must defeat these hirelings of England, inspired by such hatred of our nation!

This  ictory will conclude our campaign and we can return to winter quarters, where fresh French troops who are being raised in France will join us, and the peace I shall conclude will be worthy of my people, of you, and of myself.

NAPOLEON

CHAPTER XI 

At fi e in the morning it was still quite dark.

The troops of the center, the reser es, and Bagration's right flank had not yet mo ed, but on the left flank the columns of infantry, ca alry, and artillery, which were to be the first to descend the heights to attack the French right flank and dri e it into the Bohemian mountains according to plan, were already up and astir.

The smoke of the campfires, into which they were throwing e erything superfluous, made the eyes smart.

It was cold and dark.

The officers were hurriedly drinking tea and breakfasting, the soldiers, munching biscuit and beating a tattoo with their feet to warm themsel es, gathering round the fires throwing into the flames the remains of sheds, chairs, tables, wheels, tubs, and e erything that they did not want or could not carry away with them.

Austrian column guides were mo ing in and out among the Russian troops and ser ed as heralds of the ad ance.

As soon as an Austrian officer showed himself near a commanding officer's quarters, the regiment began to mo e  the soldiers ran from the fires, thrust their pipes into their boots, their bags into the carts, got their muskets ready, and formed rank.

The officers buttoned up their coats, buckled on their swords and pouches, and mo ed along the ranks shouting. The train dri ers and orderlies harnessed and packed the wagons and tied on the loads.

The adjutants and battalion and regimental commanders mounted, crossed themsel es, ga e final instructions, orders, and commissions to the baggage men who remained behind, and the monotonous tramp of thousands of feet resounded.

The column mo ed forward without knowing where and unable, from the masses around them, the smoke and the increasing fog, to see either the place they were lea ing or that to which they were going.

A soldier on the march is hemmed in and borne along by his regiment as much as a sailor is by his ship.

Howe er far he has walked, whate er strange, unknown, and dangerous places he reaches, just as a sailor is always surrounded by the same decks, masts, and rigging of his ship, so the soldier always has around him the same comrades, the same ranks, the same sergeant major I an Mitrich, the same company dog Jack, and the same commanders.

The sailor rarely cares to know the latitude in which his ship is sailing, but on the day of battle     hea en knows how and whence     a stern note of which all are conscious sounds in the moral atmosphere of an army, announcing the approach of something decisi e and solemn, and awakening in the men an unusual curiosity.

On the day of battle the soldiers excitedly try to get beyond the interests of their regiment, they listen intently, look about, and eagerly ask concerning what is going on around them.

The fog had grown so dense that though it was growing light they could not see ten paces ahead.

Bushes looked like gigantic trees and le el ground like cliffs and slopes.

Anywhere, on any side, one might encounter an enemy in isible ten paces off.

But the columns ad anced for a long time, always in the same fog, descending and ascending hills, a oiding gardens and enclosures, going o er new and unknown ground, and nowhere encountering the enemy.

On the contrary, the soldiers became aware that in front, behind, and on all sides, other Russian columns were mo ing in the same direction.

E ery soldier felt glad to know that to the unknown place where he was going, many more of our men were going too.

"There now, the Kurskies ha e also gone past," was being said in the ranks.

"It's wonderful what a lot of our troops ha e gathered, lads!

Last night I looked at the campfires and there was no end of them.

A regular Moscow!"

Though none of the column commanders rode up to the ranks or talked to the men (the commanders, as we saw at the council of war, were out of humor and dissatisfied with the affair, and so did not exert themsel es to cheer the men but merely carried out the orders), yet the troops marched gaily, as they always do when going into action, especially to an attack.

But when they had marched for about an hour in the dense fog, the greater part of the men had to halt and an unpleasant consciousness of some dislocation and blunder spread through the ranks.

How such a consciousness is communicated is  ery difficult to define, but it certainly is communicated  ery surely, and flows rapidly, imperceptibly, and irrepressibly, as water does in a creek.

Had the Russian army been alone without any allies, it might perhaps ha e been a long time before this consciousness of mismanagement became a general con iction, but as it was, the disorder was readily and naturally attributed to the stupid Germans, and e eryone was con inced that a dangerous muddle had been occasioned by the sausage eaters.

"Why ha e we stopped?

Is the way blocked?

Or ha e we already come up against the French?"

"No, one can't hear them.

They'd be firing if we had."

"They were in a hurry enough to start us, and now here we stand in the middle of a field without rhyme or reason. It's all those damned Germans' muddling!

What stupid de ils!"

"Yes, I'd send them on in front, but no fear, they're crowding up behind.

And now here we stand hungry."

"I say, shall we soon be clear?

They say the ca alry are blocking the way," said an officer.

"Ah, those damned Germans! They don't know their own country!" said another.

"What di ision are you?" shouted an adjutant, riding up.

"The Eighteenth."

"Then why are you here? You should ha e gone on long ago, now you won't get there till e ening."

"What stupid orders! They don't themsel es know what they are doing!" said the officer and rode off.

Then a general rode past shouting something angrily, not in Russian.

"Tafa lafa! But what he's jabbering no one can make out," said a soldier, mimicking the general who had ridden away. "I'd shoot them, the scoundrels!"

"We were ordered to be at the place before nine, but we ha en't got halfway.

Fine orders!" was being repeated on different sides.

And the feeling of energy with which the troops had started began to turn into  exation and anger at the stupid arrangements and at the Germans.

The cause of the confusion was that while the Austrian ca alry was mo ing toward our left flank, the higher command found that our center was too far separated from our right flank and the ca alry were all ordered to turn back to the right.

Se eral thousand ca alry crossed in front of the infantry, who had to wait.

At the front an altercation occurred between an Austrian guide and a Russian general.

The general shouted a demand that the ca alry should be halted, the Austrian argued that not he, but the higher command, was to blame.

The troops meanwhile stood growing listless and dispirited.

After an hour's delay they at last mo ed on, descending the hill.

The fog that was dispersing on the hill lay still more densely below, where they were descending.

In front in the fog a shot was heard and then another, at first irregularly at  arying inter als     trata... tat     and then more and more regularly and rapidly, and the action at the Goldbach Stream began.

Not expecting to come on the enemy down by the stream, and ha ing stumbled on him in the fog, hearing no encouraging word from their commanders, and with a consciousness of being too late spreading through the ranks, and abo e all being unable to see anything in front or around them in the thick fog, the Russians exchanged shots with the enemy lazily and ad anced and again halted, recei ing no timely orders from the officers or adjutants who wandered about in the fog in those unknown surroundings unable to find their own regiments.

In this way the action began for the first, second, and third columns, which had gone down into the  alley.

The fourth column, with which Kutuzo  was, stood on the Pratzen Heights.

Below, where the fight was beginning, there was still thick fog  on the higher ground it was clearing, but nothing could be seen of what was going on in front.

Whether all the enemy forces were, as we supposed, six miles away, or whether they were near by in that sea of mist, no one knew till after eight o'clock.

It was nine o'clock in the morning.

The fog lay unbroken like a sea down below, but higher up at the  illage of Schlappanitz where Napoleon stood with his marshals around him, it was quite light.

Abo e him was a clear blue sky, and the sun's  ast orb qui ered like a huge hollow, crimson float on the surface of that milky sea of mist.

The whole French army, and e en Napoleon himself with his staff, were not on the far side of the streams and hollows of Sokolnitz and Schlappanitz beyond which we intended to take up our position and begin the action, but were on this side, so close to our own forces that Napoleon with the naked eye could distinguish a mounted man from one on foot.

Napoleon, in the blue cloak which he had worn on his Italian campaign, sat on his small gray Arab horse a little in front of his marshals.

He gazed silently at the hills which seemed to rise out of the sea of mist and on which the Russian troops were mo ing in the distance, and he listened to the sounds of firing in the  alley.

Not a single muscle of his face     which in those days was still thin     mo ed. His gleaming eyes were fixed intently on one spot.

His predictions were being justified.

Part of the Russian force had already descended into the  alley toward the ponds and lakes and part were lea ing these Pratzen Heights which he intended to attack and regarded as the key to the position.

He saw o er the mist that in a hollow between two hills near the  illage of Pratzen, the Russian columns, their bayonets glittering, were mo ing continuously in one direction toward the  alley and disappearing one after another into the mist.

From information he had recei ed the e ening before, from the sound of wheels and footsteps heard by the outposts during the night, by the disorderly mo ement of the Russian columns, and from all indications, he saw clearly that the allies belie ed him to be far away in front of them, and that the columns mo ing near Pratzen constituted the center of the Russian army, and that that center was already sufficiently weakened to be successfully attacked.

But still he did not begin the engagement.

Today was a great day for him     the anni ersary of his coronation.

Before dawn he had slept for a few hours, and refreshed,  igorous, and in good spirits, he mounted his horse and rode out into the field in that happy mood in which e erything seems possible and e erything succeeds.

He sat motionless, looking at the heights  isible abo e the mist, and his cold face wore that special look of confident, self complacent happiness that one sees on the face of a boy happily in lo e.

The marshals stood behind him not  enturing to distract his attention.

He looked now at the Pratzen Heights, now at the sun floating up out of the mist.

When the sun had entirely emerged from the fog, and fields and mist were aglow with dazzling light     as if he had only awaited this to begin the action     he drew the glo e from his shapely white hand, made a sign with it to the marshals, and ordered the action to begin.

The marshals, accompanied by adjutants, galloped off in different directions, and a few minutes later the chief forces of the French army mo ed rapidly toward those Pratzen Heights which were being more and more denuded by Russian troops mo ing down the  alley to their left.

CHAPTER X 

At eight o'clock Kutuzo  rode to Pratzen at the head of the fourth column, Milorado ich's, the one that was to take the place of Przebyszewski's and Langeron's columns which had already gone down into the  alley.

He greeted the men of the foremost regiment and ga e them the order to march, thereby indicating that he intended to lead that column himself.

When he had reached the  illage of Pratzen he halted.

Prince Andrew was behind, among the immense number forming the commander in chief's suite.

He was in a state of suppressed excitement and irritation, though controlledly calm as a man is at the approach of a long awaited moment.

He was firmly con inced that this was the day of his Toulon, or his bridge of Arcola.

How it would come about he did not know, but he felt sure it would do so.

The locality and the position of our troops were known to him as far as they could be known to anyone in our army.

His own strategic plan, which ob iously could not now be carried out, was forgotten.

Now, entering into Weyrother's plan, Prince Andrew considered possible contingencies and formed new projects such as might call for his rapidity of perception and decision.

To the left down below in the mist, the musketry fire of unseen forces could be heard.

It was there Prince Andrew thought the fight would concentrate. "There we shall encounter difficulties, and there," thought he, "I shall be sent with a brigade or di ision, and there, standard in hand, I shall go forward and break whate er is in front of me."

He could not look calmly at the standards of the passing battalions.

Seeing them he kept thinking, "That may be the  ery standard with which I shall lead the army."

In the morning all that was left of the night mist on the heights was a hoar frost now turning to dew, but in the  alleys it still lay like a milk white sea.

Nothing was  isible in the  alley to the left into which our troops had descended and from whence came the sounds of firing.

Abo e the heights was the dark clear sky, and to the right the  ast orb of the sun.

In front, far off on the farther shore of that sea of mist, some wooded hills were discernible, and it was there the enemy probably was, for something could be descried.

On the right the Guards were entering the misty region with a sound of hoofs and wheels and now and then a gleam of bayonets  to the left beyond the  illage similar masses of ca alry came up and disappeared in the sea of mist.

In front and behind mo ed infantry.

The commander in chief was standing at the end of the  illage letting the troops pass by him.

That morning Kutuzo  seemed worn and irritable.

The infantry passing before him came to a halt without any command being gi en, apparently obstructed by something in front.

"Do order them to form into battalion columns and go round the  illage!" he said angrily to a general who had ridden up. "Don't you understand, your excellency, my dear sir, that you must not defile through narrow  illage streets when we are marching against the enemy?"

"I intended to re form them beyond the  illage, your excellency," answered the general.

Kutuzo  laughed bitterly.

"You'll make a fine thing of it, deploying in sight of the enemy!  ery fine!"

"The enemy is still far away, your excellency.

According to the dispositions..."

"The dispositions!" exclaimed Kutuzo  bitterly. "Who told you that?... Kindly do as you are ordered."

"Yes, sir."

"My dear fellow," Nes itski whispered to Prince Andrew, "the old man is as surly as a dog."

An Austrian officer in a white uniform with green plumes in his hat galloped up to Kutuzo  and asked in the Emperor's name had the fourth column ad anced into action.

Kutuzo  turned round without answering and his eye happened to fall upon Prince Andrew, who was beside him.

Seeing him, Kutuzo 's male olent and caustic expression softened, as if admitting that what was being done was not his adjutant's fault, and still not answering the Austrian adjutant, he addressed Bolkonski.

"Go, my dear fellow, and see whether the third di ision has passed the  illage.

Tell it to stop and await my orders."

Hardly had Prince Andrew started than he stopped him.

"And ask whether sharpshooters ha e been posted," he added. "What are they doing? What are they doing?" he murmured to himself, still not replying to the Austrian.

Prince Andrew galloped off to execute the order.

O ertaking the battalions that continued to ad ance, he stopped the third di ision and con inced himself that there really were no sharpshooters in front of our columns.

The colonel at the head of the regiment was much surprised at the commander in chief's order to throw out skirmishers.

He had felt perfectly sure that there were other troops in front of him and that the enemy must be at least six miles away.

There was really nothing to be seen in front except a barren descent hidden by dense mist.

Ha ing gi en orders in the commander in chief's name to rectify this omission, Prince Andrew galloped back.

Kutuzo  still in the same place, his stout body resting hea ily in the saddle with the lassitude of age, sat yawning wearily with closed eyes.

The troops were no longer mo ing, but stood with the butts of their muskets on the ground.

"All right, all right!" he said to Prince Andrew, and turned to a general who, watch in hand, was saying it was time they started as all the left flank columns had already descended.

"Plenty of time, your excellency," muttered Kutuzo  in the midst of a yawn. "Plenty of time," he repeated.

Just then at a distance behind Kutuzo  was heard the sound of regiments saluting, and this sound rapidly came nearer along the whole extended line of the ad ancing Russian columns.

E idently the person they were greeting was riding quickly.

When the soldiers of the regiment in front of which Kutuzo  was standing began to shout, he rode a little to one side and looked round with a frown.

Along the road from Pratzen galloped what looked like a squadron of horsemen in  arious uniforms.

Two of them rode side by side in front, at full gallop.

One in a black uniform with white plumes in his hat rode a bobtailed chestnut horse, the other who was in a white uniform rode a black one.

These were the two Emperors followed by their suites.

Kutuzo , affecting the manners of an old soldier at the front, ga e the command "Attention!" and rode up to the Emperors with a salute.

His whole appearance and manner were suddenly transformed.

He put on the air of a subordinate who obeys without reasoning.

With an affectation of respect which e idently struck Alexander unpleasantly, he rode up and saluted.

This unpleasant impression merely flitted o er the young and happy face of the Emperor like a cloud of haze across a clear sky and  anished.

After his illness he looked rather thinner that day than on the field of Olmutz where Bolkonski had seen him for the first time abroad, but there was still the same bewitching combination of majesty and mildness in his fine gray eyes, and on his delicate lips the same capacity for  arying expression and the same pre alent appearance of goodhearted innocent youth.

At the Olmutz re iew he had seemed more majestic  here he seemed brighter and more energetic.

He was slightly flushed after galloping two miles, and reining in his horse he sighed restfully and looked round at the faces of his suite, young and animated as his own.

Czartoryski, No osiltse , Prince  olkonsky, Strogono , and the others, all richly dressed gay young men on splendid, well groomed, fresh, only slightly heated horses, exchanging remarks and smiling, had stopped behind the Emperor.

The Emperor Francis, a rosy, long faced young man, sat  ery erect on his handsome black horse, looking about him in a leisurely and preoccupied manner.

He beckoned to one of his white adjutants and asked some question     "Most likely he is asking at what o'clock they started," thought Prince Andrew, watching his old acquaintance with a smile he could not repress as he recalled his reception at Brunn.

In the Emperors' suite were the picked young orderly officers of the Guard and line regiments, Russian and Austrian.

Among them were grooms leading the Tsar's beautiful relay horses co ered with embroidered cloths.

As when a window is opened a whiff of fresh air from the fields enters a stuffy room, so a whiff of youthfulness, energy, and confidence of success reached Kutuzo 's cheerless staff with the galloping ad ent of all these brilliant young men.

"Why aren't you beginning, Michael Ilariono ich?" said the Emperor Alexander hurriedly to Kutuzo , glancing courteously at the same time at the Emperor Francis.

"I am waiting, Your Majesty," answered Kutuzo , bending forward respectfully.

The Emperor, frowning slightly, bent his ear forward as if he had not quite heard.

"Waiting, Your Majesty," repeated Kutuzo . (Prince Andrew noted that Kutuzo 's upper lip twitched unnaturally as he said the word "waiting.") "Not all the columns ha e formed up yet, Your Majesty."

The Tsar heard but ob iously did not like the reply  he shrugged his rather round shoulders and glanced at No osiltse  who was near him, as if complaining of Kutuzo .

"You know, Michael Ilariono ich, we are not on the Empress' Field where a parade does not begin till all the troops are assembled," said the Tsar with another glance at the Emperor Francis, as if in iting him if not to join in at least to listen to what he was saying. But the Emperor Francis continued to look about him and did not listen.

"That is just why I do not begin, sire," said Kutuzo  in a resounding  oice, apparently to preclude the possibility of not being heard, and again something in his face twitched     "That is just why I do not begin, sire, because we are not on parade and not on the Empress' Field," said clearly and distinctly.

In the Emperor's suite all exchanged rapid looks that expressed dissatisfaction and reproach.

"Old though he may be, he should not, he certainly should not, speak like that," their glances seemed to say.

The Tsar looked intently and obser antly into Kutuzo 's eye waiting to hear whether he would say anything more.

But Kutuzo , with respectfully bowed head, seemed also to be waiting.

The silence lasted for about a minute.

"Howe er, if you command it, Your Majesty," said Kutuzo , lifting his head and again assuming his former tone of a dull, unreasoning, but submissi e general.

He touched his horse and ha ing called Milorado ich, the commander of the column, ga e him the order to ad ance.

The troops again began to mo e, and two battalions of the No gorod and one of the Apsheron regiment went forward past the Emperor.

As this Apsheron battalion marched by, the red faced Milorado ich, without his greatcoat, with his Orders on his breast and an enormous tuft of plumes in his cocked hat worn on one side with its corners front and back, galloped strenuously forward, and with a dashing salute reined in his horse before the Emperor.

"God be with you, general!" said the Emperor.

"Ma foi, sire, nous ferons ce qui sera dans notre possibilite, sire,"   he answered gaily, raising ne ertheless ironic smiles among the gentlemen of the Tsar's suite by his poor French.   "Indeed, Sire, we shall do e erything it is possible to do, Sire."

Milorado ich wheeled his horse sharply and stationed himself a little behind the Emperor.

The Apsheron men, excited by the Tsar's presence, passed in step before the Emperors and their suites at a bold, brisk pace.

"Lads!" shouted Milorado ich in a loud, self confident, and cheery  oice, ob iously so elated by the sound of firing, by the prospect of battle, and by the sight of the gallant Apsherons, his comrades in Su oro 's time, now passing so gallantly before the Emperors, that he forgot the so ereigns' presence. "Lads, it's not the first  illage you' e had to take," cried he.

"Glad to do our best!" shouted the soldiers.

The Emperor's horse started at the sudden cry.

This horse that had carried the so ereign at re iews in Russia bore him also here on the field of Austerlitz, enduring the heedless blows of his left foot and pricking its ears at the sound of shots just as it had done on the Empress' Field, not understanding the significance of the firing, nor of the nearness of the Emperor Francis' black cob, nor of all that was being said, thought, and felt that day by its rider.

The Emperor turned with a smile to one of his followers and made a remark to him, pointing to the gallant Apsherons.

CHAPTER X I

Kutuzo  accompanied by his adjutants rode at a walking pace behind the carabineers.

When he had gone less than half a mile in the rear of the column he stopped at a solitary, deserted house that had probably once been an inn, where two roads parted.

Both of them led downhill and troops were marching along both.

The fog had begun to clear and enemy troops were already dimly  isible about a mile and a half off on the opposite heights.

Down below, on the left, the firing became more distinct.

Kutuzo  had stopped and was speaking to an Austrian general.

Prince Andrew, who was a little behind looking at them, turned to an adjutant to ask him for a field glass.

"Look, look!" said this adjutant, looking not at the troops in the distance, but down the hill before him. "It's the French!"

The two generals and the adjutant took hold of the field glass, trying to snatch it from one another.

The expression on all their faces suddenly changed to one of horror.

The French were supposed to be a mile and a half away, but had suddenly and unexpectedly appeared just in front of us.

"It's the enemy?... No!... Yes, see it is!... for certain.... But how is that?" said different  oices.

With the naked eye Prince Andrew saw below them to the right, not more than fi e hundred paces from where Kutuzo  was standing, a dense French column coming up to meet the Apsherons.

"Here it is! The decisi e moment has arri ed.

My turn has come," thought Prince Andrew, and striking his horse he rode up to Kutuzo .

"The Apsherons must be stopped, your excellency," cried he.

But at that  ery instant a cloud of smoke spread all round, firing was heard quite close at hand, and a  oice of nai e terror barely two steps from Prince Andrew shouted, "Brothers! All's lost!"

And at this as if at a command, e eryone began to run.

Confused and e er increasing crowds were running back to where fi e minutes before the troops had passed the Emperors.

Not only would it ha e been difficult to stop that crowd, it was e en impossible not to be carried back with it oneself.

Bolkonski only tried not to lose touch with it, and looked around bewildered and unable to grasp what was happening in front of him.

Nes itski with an angry face, red and unlike himself, was shouting to Kutuzo  that if he did not ride away at once he would certainly be taken prisoner.

Kutuzo  remained in the same place and without answering drew out a handkerchief.

Blood was flowing from his cheek.

Prince Andrew forced his way to him.

"You are wounded?" he asked, hardly able to master the trembling of his lower jaw.

"The wound is not here, it is there!" said Kutuzo , pressing the handkerchief to his wounded cheek and pointing to the fleeing soldiers. "Stop them!" he shouted, and at the same moment, probably realizing that it was impossible to stop them, spurred his horse and rode to the right.

A fresh wa e of the flying mob caught him and bore him back with it.

The troops were running in such a dense mass that once surrounded by them it was difficult to get out again.

One was shouting,

"Get on! Why are you hindering us?"

Another in the same place turned round and fired in the air  a third was striking the horse Kutuzo  himself rode.

Ha ing by a great effort got away to the left from that flood of men, Kutuzo , with his suite diminished by more than half, rode toward a sound of artillery fire near by.

Ha ing forced his way out of the crowd of fugiti es, Prince Andrew, trying to keep near Kutuzo , saw on the slope of the hill amid the smoke a Russian battery that was still firing and Frenchmen running toward it.

Higher up stood some Russian infantry, neither mo ing forward to protect the battery nor backward with the fleeing crowd.

A mounted general separated himself from the infantry and approached Kutuzo .

Of Kutuzo 's suite only four remained.

They were all pale and exchanged looks in silence.

"Stop those wretches!" gasped Kutuzo  to the regimental commander, pointing to the flying soldiers  but at that instant, as if to punish him for those words, bullets flew hissing across the regiment and across Kutuzo 's suite like a flock of little birds.

The French had attacked the battery and, seeing Kutuzo , were firing at him.

After this  olley the regimental commander clutched at his leg  se eral soldiers fell, and a second lieutenant who was holding the flag let it fall from his hands. It swayed and fell, but caught on the muskets of the nearest soldiers.

The soldiers started firing without orders.

"Oh! Oh! Oh!" groaned Kutuzo  despairingly and looked around.... "Bolkonski!" he whispered, his  oice trembling from a consciousness of the feebleness of age, "Bolkonski!" he whispered, pointing to the disordered battalion and at the enemy, "what's that?"

But before he had finished speaking, Prince Andrew, feeling tears of shame and anger choking him, had already leapt from his horse and run to the standard.

"Forward, lads!" he shouted in a  oice piercing as a child's.

"Here it is!" thought he, seizing the staff of the standard and hearing with pleasure the whistle of bullets e idently aimed at him.

Se eral soldiers fell.

"Hurrah!" shouted Prince Andrew, and, scarcely able to hold up the hea y standard, he ran forward with full confidence that the whole battalion would follow him.

And really he only ran a few steps alone.

One soldier mo ed and then another and soon the whole battalion ran forward shouting "Hurrah!" and o ertook him.

A sergeant of the battalion ran up and took the flag that was swaying from its weight in Prince Andrew's hands, but he was immediately killed.

Prince Andrew again seized the standard and, dragging it by the staff, ran on with the battalion.

In front he saw our artillerymen, some of whom were fighting, while others, ha ing abandoned their guns, were running toward him. He also saw French infantry soldiers who were seizing the artillery horses and turning the guns round.

Prince Andrew and the battalion were already within twenty paces of the cannon.

He heard the whistle of bullets abo e him unceasingly and to right and left of him soldiers continually groaned and dropped.

But he did not look at them  he looked only at what was going on in front of him     at the battery.

He now saw clearly the figure of a red haired gunner with his shako knocked awry, pulling one end of a mop while a French soldier tugged at the other.

He could distinctly see the distraught yet angry expression on the faces of these two men, who e idently did not realize what they were doing.

"What are they about?" thought Prince Andrew as he gazed at them. "Why doesn't the red haired gunner run away as he is unarmed?

Why doesn't the Frenchman stab him?

He will not get away before the Frenchman remembers his bayonet and stabs him...."

And really another French soldier, trailing his musket, ran up to the struggling men, and the fate of the red haired gunner, who had triumphantly secured the mop and still did not realize what awaited him, was about to be decided.

But Prince Andrew did not see how it ended.

It seemed to him as though one of the soldiers near him hit him on the head with the full swing of a bludgeon.

It hurt a little, but the worst of it was that the pain distracted him and pre ented his seeing what he had been looking at.

"What's this? Am I falling? My legs are gi ing way," thought he, and fell on his back.

He opened his eyes, hoping to see how the struggle of the Frenchmen with the gunners ended, whether the red haired gunner had been killed or not and whether the cannon had been captured or sa ed.

But he saw nothing.

Abo e him there was now nothing but the sky     the lofty sky, not clear yet still immeasurably lofty, with gray clouds gliding slowly across it.

"How quiet, peaceful, and solemn  not at all as I ran," thought Prince Andrew     "not as we ran, shouting and fighting, not at all as the gunner and the Frenchman with frightened and angry faces struggled for the mop  how differently do those clouds glide across that lofty infinite sky!
