How was it I did not see that lofty sky before?

And how happy I am to ha e found it at last!

Yes! All is  anity, all falsehood, except that infinite sky.

There is nothing, nothing, but that.

But e en it does not exist, there is nothing but quiet and peace.

Thank God!..."

CHAPTER X II

On our right flank commanded by Bagration, at nine o'clock the battle had not yet begun.

Not wishing to agree to Dolgoruko 's demand to commence the action, and wishing to a ert responsibility from himself, Prince Bagration proposed to Dolgoruko  to send to inquire of the commander in chief.

Bagration knew that as the distance between the two flanks was more than six miles, e en if the messenger were not killed (which he  ery likely would be), and found the commander in chief (which would be  ery difficult), he would not be able to get back before e ening.

Bagration cast his large, expressionless, sleepy eyes round his suite, and the boyish face Rosto , breathless with excitement and hope, was the first to catch his eye.

He sent him.

"And if I should meet His Majesty before I meet the commander in chief, your excellency?" said Rosto , with his hand to his cap.

"You can gi e the message to His Majesty," said Dolgoruko , hurriedly interrupting Bagration.

On being relie ed from picket duty Rosto  had managed to get a few hours' sleep before morning and felt cheerful, bold, and resolute, with elasticity of mo ement, faith in his good fortune, and generally in that state of mind which makes e erything seem possible, pleasant, and easy.

All his wishes were being fulfilled that morning  there was to be a general engagement in which he was taking part, more than that, he was orderly to the bra est general, and still more, he was going with a message to Kutuzo , perhaps e en to the so ereign himself.

The morning was bright, he had a good horse under him, and his heart was full of joy and happiness.

On recei ing the order he ga e his horse the rein and galloped along the line.

At first he rode along the line of Bagration's troops, which had not yet ad anced into action but were standing motionless  then he came to the region occupied by U aro 's ca alry and here he noticed a stir and signs of preparation for battle  ha ing passed U aro 's ca alry he clearly heard the sound of cannon and musketry ahead of him.

The firing grew louder and louder.

In the fresh morning air were now heard, not two or three musket shots at irregular inter als as before, followed by one or two cannon shots, but a roll of  olleys of musketry from the slopes of the hill before Pratzen, interrupted by such frequent reports of cannon that sometimes se eral of them were not separated from one another but merged into a general roar.

He could see puffs of musketry smoke that seemed to chase one another down the hillsides, and clouds of cannon smoke rolling, spreading, and mingling with one another.

He could also, by the gleam of bayonets  isible through the smoke, make out mo ing masses of infantry and narrow lines of artillery with green caissons.

Rosto  stopped his horse for a moment on a hillock to see what was going on, but strain his attention as he would he could not understand or make out anything of what was happening  there in the smoke men of some sort were mo ing about, in front and behind mo ed lines of troops  but why, whither, and who they were, it was impossible to make out.

These sights and sounds had no depressing or intimidating effect on him  on the contrary, they stimulated his energy and determination.

"Go on! Go on! Gi e it them!" he mentally exclaimed at these sounds, and again proceeded to gallop along the line, penetrating farther and farther into the region where the army was already in action.

"How it will be there I don't know, but all will be well!" thought Rosto .

After passing some Austrian troops he noticed that the next part of the line (the Guards) was already in action.

"So much the better! I shall see it close," he thought.

He was riding almost along the front line.

A handful of men came galloping toward him.

They were our Uhlans who with disordered ranks were returning from the attack.

Rosto  got out of their way, in oluntarily noticed that one of them was bleeding, and galloped on.

"That is no business of mine," he thought.

He had not ridden many hundred yards after that before he saw to his left, across the whole width of the field, an enormous mass of ca alry in brilliant white uniforms, mounted on black horses, trotting straight toward him and across his path.

Rosto  put his horse to full gallop to get out of the way of these men, and he would ha e got clear had they continued at the same speed, but they kept increasing their pace, so that some of the horses were already galloping.

Rosto  heard the thud of their hoofs and the jingle of their weapons and saw their horses, their figures, and e en their faces, more and more distinctly.

They were our Horse Guards, ad ancing to attack the French ca alry that was coming to meet them.

The Horse Guards were galloping, but still holding in their horses.

Rosto  could already see their faces and heard the command  "Charge!" shouted by an officer who was urging his thoroughbred to full speed.

Rosto , fearing to be crushed or swept into the attack on the French, galloped along the front as hard as his horse could go, but still was not in time to a oid them.

The last of the Horse Guards, a huge pockmarked fellow, frowned angrily on seeing Rosto  before him, with whom he would ine itably collide.

This Guardsman would certainly ha e bowled Rosto  and his Bedouin o er (Rosto  felt himself quite tiny and weak compared to these gigantic men and horses) had it not occurred to Rosto  to flourish his whip before the eyes of the Guardsman's horse.

The hea y black horse, sixteen hands high, shied, throwing back its ears  but the pockmarked Guardsman dro e his huge spurs in  iolently, and the horse, flourishing its tail and extending its neck, galloped on yet faster.

Hardly had the Horse Guards passed Rosto  before he heard them shout,

"Hurrah!" and looking back saw that their foremost ranks were mixed up with some foreign ca alry with red epaulets, probably French.

He could see nothing more, for immediately afterwards cannon began firing from somewhere and smoke en eloped e erything.

At that moment, as the Horse Guards, ha ing passed him, disappeared in the smoke, Rosto  hesitated whether to gallop after them or to go where he was sent.

This was the brilliant charge of the Horse Guards that amazed the French themsel es.

Rosto  was horrified to hear later that of all that mass of huge and handsome men, of all those brilliant, rich youths, officers and cadets, who had galloped past him on their thousand ruble horses, only eighteen were left after the charge.

"Why should I en y them? My chance is not lost, and maybe I shall see the Emperor immediately!" thought Rosto  and galloped on.

When he came le el with the Foot Guards he noticed that about them and around them cannon balls were flying, of which he was aware not so much because he heard their sound as because he saw uneasiness on the soldiers' faces and unnatural warlike solemnity on those of the officers.

Passing behind one of the lines of a regiment of Foot Guards he heard a  oice calling him by name.

"Rosto !"

"What?" he answered, not recognizing Boris.

"I say, we' e been in the front line!

Our regiment attacked!" said Boris with the happy smile seen on the faces of young men who ha e been under fire for the first time.

Rosto  stopped.

"Ha e you?" he said. "Well, how did it go?"

"We dro e them back!" said Boris with animation, growing talkati e. "Can you imagine it?" and he began describing how the Guards, ha ing taken up their position and seeing troops before them, thought they were Austrians, and all at once disco ered from the cannon balls discharged by those troops that they were themsel es in the front line and had unexpectedly to go into action.

Rosto  without hearing Boris to the end spurred his horse.

"Where are you off to?" asked Boris.

"With a message to His Majesty."

"There he is!" said Boris, thinking Rosto  had said "His Highness," and pointing to the Grand Duke who with his high shoulders and frowning brows stood a hundred paces away from them in his helmet and Horse Guards' jacket, shouting something to a pale, white uniformed Austrian officer.

"But that's the Grand Duke, and I want the commander in chief or the Emperor," said Rosto , and was about to spur his horse.

"Count! Count!" shouted Berg who ran up from the other side as eager as Boris. "Count! I am wounded in my right hand" (and he showed his bleeding hand with a handkerchief tied round it) "and I remained at the front.

I held my sword in my left hand, Count. All our family     the  on Bergs     ha e been knights!"

He said something more, but Rosto  did not wait to hear it and rode away.

Ha ing passed the Guards and tra ersed an empty space, Rosto , to a oid again getting in front of the first line as he had done when the Horse Guards charged, followed the line of reser es, going far round the place where the hottest musket fire and cannonade were heard.

Suddenly he heard musket fire quite close in front of him and behind our troops, where he could ne er ha e expected the enemy to be.

"What can it be?" he thought. "The enemy in the rear of our army?

Impossible!" And suddenly he was seized by a panic of fear for himself and for the issue of the whole battle. "But be that what it may," he reflected, "there is no riding round it now.

I must look for the commander in chief here, and if all is lost it is for me to perish with the rest."

The foreboding of e il that had suddenly come o er Rosto  was more and more confirmed the farther he rode into the region behind the  illage of Pratzen, which was full of troops of all kinds.

"What does it mean?

What is it?

Whom are they firing at?

Who is firing?" Rosto  kept asking as he came up to Russian and Austrian soldiers running in confused crowds across his path.

"The de il knows!

They' e killed e erybody!

It's all up now!" he was told in Russian, German, and Czech by the crowd of fugiti es who understood what was happening as little as he did.

"Kill the Germans!" shouted one.

"May the de il take them     the traitors!"

"Zum Henker diese Russen!"   muttered a German.   "Hang these Russians!"

Se eral wounded men passed along the road, and words of abuse, screams, and groans mingled in a general hubbub, then the firing died down.

Rosto  learned later that Russian and Austrian soldiers had been firing at one another.

"My God! What does it all mean?" thought he. "And here, where at any moment the Emperor may see them.... But no, these must be only a handful of scoundrels.

It will soon be o er, it can't be that, it can't be! Only to get past them quicker, quicker!"

The idea of defeat and flight could not enter Rosto 's head.

Though he saw French cannon and French troops on the Pratzen Heights just where he had been ordered to look for the commander in chief, he could not, did not wish to, belie e that.

CHAPTER X III

Rosto  had been ordered to look for Kutuzo  and the Emperor near the  illage of Pratzen.

But neither they nor a single commanding officer were there, only disorganized crowds of troops of  arious kinds.

He urged on his already weary horse to get quickly past these crowds, but the farther he went the more disorganized they were.

The highroad on which he had come out was thronged with caleches, carriages of all sorts, and Russian and Austrian soldiers of all arms, some wounded and some not.

This whole mass droned and jostled in confusion under the dismal influence of cannon balls flying from the French batteries stationed on the Pratzen Heights.

"Where is the Emperor? Where is Kutuzo ?" Rosto  kept asking e eryone he could stop, but got no answer from anyone.

At last seizing a soldier by his collar he forced him to answer.

"Eh, brother!

They' e all bolted long ago!" said the soldier, laughing for some reason and shaking himself free.

Ha ing left that soldier who was e idently drunk, Rosto  stopped the horse of a batman or groom of some important personage and began to question him.

The man announced that the Tsar had been dri en in a carriage at full speed about an hour before along that  ery road and that he was dangerously wounded.

"It can't be!" said Rosto . "It must ha e been someone else."

"I saw him myself," replied the man with a self confident smile of derision. "I ought to know the Emperor by now, after the times I' e seen him in Petersburg. I saw him just as I see you....

There he sat in the carriage as pale as anything.

How they made the four black horses fly! Gracious me, they did rattle past! It's time I knew the Imperial horses and Ilya I anych. I don't think Ilya dri es anyone except the Tsar!"

Rosto  let go of the horse and was about to ride on, when a wounded officer passing by addressed him 

"Who is it you want?" he asked. "The commander in chief?

He was killed by a cannon ball     struck in the breast before our regiment."

"Not killed     wounded!" another officer corrected him.

"Who?

Kutuzo ?" asked Rosto .

"Not Kutuzo , but what's his name     well, ne er mind... there are not many left ali e.

Go that way, to that  illage, all the commanders are there," said the officer, pointing to the  illage of Hosjeradek, and he walked on.

Rosto  rode on at a footpace not knowing why or to whom he was now going.

The Emperor was wounded, the battle lost.

It was impossible to doubt it now.

Rosto  rode in the direction pointed out to him, in which he saw turrets and a church.

What need to hurry?

What was he now to say to the Tsar or to Kutuzo , e en if they were ali e and unwounded?

"Take this road, your honor, that way you will be killed at once!" a soldier shouted to him. "They'd kill you there!"

"Oh, what are you talking about?" said another. "Where is he to go?

That way is nearer."

Rosto  considered, and then went in the direction where they said he would be killed.

"It's all the same now. If the Emperor is wounded, am I to try to sa e myself?" he thought.

He rode on to the region where the greatest number of men had perished in fleeing from Pratzen.

The French had not yet occupied that region, and the Russians     the uninjured and slightly wounded     had left it long ago.

All about the field, like heaps of manure on well kept plowland, lay from ten to fifteen dead and wounded to each couple of acres.

The wounded crept together in twos and threes and one could hear their distressing screams and groans, sometimes feigned     or so it seemed to Rosto .

He put his horse to a trot to a oid seeing all these suffering men, and he felt afraid     afraid not for his life, but for the courage he needed and which he knew would not stand the sight of these unfortunates.

The French, who had ceased firing at this field strewn with dead and wounded where there was no one left to fire at, on seeing an adjutant riding o er it trained a gun on him and fired se eral shots.

The sensation of those terrible whistling sounds and of the corpses around him merged in Rosto 's mind into a single feeling of terror and pity for himself.

He remembered his mother's last letter.

"What would she feel," thought he, "if she saw me here now on this field with the cannon aimed at me?"

In the  illage of Hosjeradek there were Russian troops retiring from the field of battle, who though still in some confusion were less disordered.

The French cannon did not reach there and the musketry fire sounded far away.

Here e eryone clearly saw and said that the battle was lost.

No one whom Rosto  asked could tell him where the Emperor or Kutuzo  was.

Some said the report that the Emperor was wounded was correct, others that it was not, and explained the false rumor that had spread by the fact that the Emperor's carriage had really galloped from the field of battle with the pale and terrified Ober Hofmarschal Count Tolstoy, who had ridden out to the battlefield with others in the Emperor's suite.

One officer told Rosto  that he had seen someone from headquarters behind the  illage to the left, and thither Rosto  rode, not hoping to find anyone but merely to ease his conscience.

When he had ridden about two miles and had passed the last of the Russian troops, he saw, near a kitchen garden with a ditch round it, two men on horseback facing the ditch.

One with a white plume in his hat seemed familiar to Rosto   the other on a beautiful chestnut horse (which Rosto  fancied he had seen before) rode up to the ditch, struck his horse with his spurs, and gi ing it the rein leaped lightly o er.

Only a little earth crumbled from the bank under the horse's hind hoofs.

Turning the horse sharply, he again jumped the ditch, and deferentially addressed the horseman with the white plumes, e idently suggesting that he should do the same.

The rider, whose figure seemed familiar to Rosto  and in oluntarily ri eted his attention, made a gesture of refusal with his head and hand and by that gesture Rosto  instantly recognized his lamented and adored monarch.

"But it can't be he, alone in the midst of this empty field!" thought Rosto .

At that moment Alexander turned his head and Rosto  saw the belo ed features that were so deeply engra ed on his memory.

The Emperor was pale, his cheeks sunken and his eyes hollow, but the charm, the mildness of his features, was all the greater.

Rosto  was happy in the assurance that the rumors about the Emperor being wounded were false.

He was happy to be seeing him.

He knew that he might and e en ought to go straight to him and gi e the message Dolgoruko  had ordered him to deli er.

But as a youth in lo e trembles, is unner ed, and dares not utter the thoughts he has dreamed of for nights, but looks around for help or a chance of delay and flight when the longed for moment comes and he is alone with her, so Rosto , now that he had attained what he had longed for more than anything else in the world, did not know how to approach the Emperor, and a thousand reasons occurred to him why it would be incon enient, unseemly, and impossible to do so.

"What!

It is as if I were glad of a chance to take ad antage of his being alone and despondent!

A strange face may seem unpleasant or painful to him at this moment of sorrow  besides, what can I say to him now, when my heart fails me and my mouth feels dry at the mere sight of him?"

Not one of the innumerable speeches addressed to the Emperor that he had composed in his imagination could he now recall.

Those speeches were intended for quite other conditions, they were for the most part to be spoken at a moment of  ictory and triumph, generally when he was dying of wounds and the so ereign had thanked him for heroic deeds, and while dying he expressed the lo e his actions had pro ed.

"Besides how can I ask the Emperor for his instructions for the right flank now that it is nearly four o'clock and the battle is lost?

No, certainly I must not approach him, I must not intrude on his reflections.

Better die a thousand times than risk recei ing an unkind look or bad opinion from him," Rosto  decided  and sorrowfully and with a heart full despair he rode away, continually looking back at the Tsar, who still remained in the same attitude of indecision.

While Rosto  was thus arguing with himself and riding sadly away, Captain  on Toll chanced to ride to the same spot, and seeing the Emperor at once rode up to him, offered his ser ices, and assisted him to cross the ditch on foot.

The Emperor, wishing to rest and feeling unwell, sat down under an apple tree and  on Toll remained beside him.

Rosto  from a distance saw with en y and remorse how  on Toll spoke long and warmly to the Emperor and how the Emperor, e idently weeping, co ered his eyes with his hand and pressed  on Toll's hand.

"And I might ha e been in his place!" thought Rosto , and hardly restraining his tears of pity for the Emperor, he rode on in utter despair, not knowing where to or why he was now riding.

His despair was all the greater from feeling that his own weakness was the cause of his grief.

He might... not only might but should, ha e gone up to the so ereign.

It was a unique chance to show his de otion to the Emperor and he had not made use of it....

"What ha e I done?" thought he.

And he turned round and galloped back to the place where he had seen the Emperor, but there was no one beyond the ditch now.

Only some carts and carriages were passing by.

From one of the dri ers he learned that Kutuzo 's staff were not far off, in the  illage the  ehicles were going to.

Rosto  followed them.

In front of him walked Kutuzo 's groom leading horses in horsecloths.

Then came a cart, and behind that walked an old, bandy legged domestic serf in a peaked cap and sheepskin coat.

"Tit! I say, Tit!" said the groom.

"What?" answered the old man absent mindedly.

"Go, Tit!

Thresh a bit!"

"Oh, you fool!" said the old man, spitting angrily.

Some time passed in silence, and then the same joke was repeated.

Before fi e in the e ening the battle had been lost at all points.

More than a hundred cannon were already in the hands of the French.

Przebyszewski and his corps had laid down their arms.

Other columns after losing half their men were retreating in disorderly confused masses.

The remains of Langeron's and Dokhturo 's mingled forces were crowding around the dams and banks of the ponds near the  illage of Augesd.

After fi e o'clock it was only at the Augesd Dam that a hot cannonade (deli ered by the French alone) was still to be heard from numerous batteries ranged on the slopes of the Pratzen Heights, directed at our retreating forces.

In the rearguard, Dokhturo  and others rallying some battalions kept up a musketry fire at the French ca alry that was pursuing our troops.

It was growing dusk.

On the narrow Augesd Dam where for so many years the old miller had been accustomed to sit in his tasseled cap peacefully angling, while his grandson, with shirt slee es rolled up, handled the floundering sil ery fish in the watering can, on that dam o er which for so many years Mora ians in shaggy caps and blue jackets had peacefully dri en their two horse carts loaded with wheat and had returned dusty with flour whitening their carts     on that narrow dam amid the wagons and the cannon, under the horses' hoofs and between the wagon wheels, men disfigured by fear of death now crowded together, crushing one another, dying, stepping o er the dying and killing one another, only to mo e on a few steps and be killed themsel es in the same way.

E ery ten seconds a cannon ball flew compressing the air around, or a shell burst in the midst of that dense throng, killing some and splashing with blood those near them.

Dolokho      now an officer     wounded in the arm, and on foot, with the regimental commander on horseback and some ten men of his company, represented all that was left of that whole regiment.

Impelled by the crowd, they had got wedged in at the approach to the dam and, jammed in on all sides, had stopped because a horse in front had fallen under a cannon and the crowd were dragging it out.

A cannon ball killed someone behind them, another fell in front and splashed Dolokho  with blood.

The crowd, pushing forward desperately, squeezed together, mo ed a few steps, and again stopped.

"Mo e on a hundred yards and we are certainly sa ed, remain here another two minutes and it is certain death," thought each one.

Dolokho  who was in the midst of the crowd forced his way to the edge of the dam, throwing two soldiers off their feet, and ran onto the slippery ice that co ered the millpool.

"Turn this way!" he shouted, jumping o er the ice which creaked under him  "turn this way!" he shouted to those with the gun. "It bears!..."

The ice bore him but it swayed and creaked, and it was plain that it would gi e way not only under a cannon or a crowd, but  ery soon e en under his weight alone.

The men looked at him and pressed to the bank, hesitating to step onto the ice.

The general on horseback at the entrance to the dam raised his hand and opened his mouth to address Dolokho .

Suddenly a cannon ball hissed so low abo e the crowd that e eryone ducked.

It flopped into something moist, and the general fell from his horse in a pool of blood.

Nobody ga e him a look or thought of raising him.

"Get onto the ice, o er the ice!

Go on! Turn! Don't you hear?

Go on!" innumerable  oices suddenly shouted after the ball had struck the general, the men themsel es not knowing what, or why, they were shouting.

One of the hindmost guns that was going onto the dam turned off onto the ice.

Crowds of soldiers from the dam began running onto the frozen pond.

The ice ga e way under one of the foremost soldiers, and one leg slipped into the water. He tried to right himself but fell in up to his waist.

The nearest soldiers shrank back, the gun dri er stopped his horse, but from behind still came the shouts 

"Onto the ice, why do you stop? Go on! Go on!"

And cries of horror were heard in the crowd.

The soldiers near the gun wa ed their arms and beat the horses to make them turn and mo e on.

The horses mo ed off the bank.

The ice, that had held under those on foot, collapsed in a great mass, and some forty men who were on it dashed, some forward and some back, drowning one another.

Still the cannon balls continued regularly to whistle and flop onto the ice and into the water and oftenest of all among the crowd that co ered the dam, the pond, and the bank.

CHAPTER XIX

On the Pratzen Heights, where he had fallen with the flagstaff in his hand, lay Prince Andrew Bolkonski bleeding profusely and unconsciously uttering a gentle, piteous, and childlike moan.

Toward e ening he ceased moaning and became quite still.

He did not know how long his unconsciousness lasted.

Suddenly he again felt that he was ali e and suffering from a burning, lacerating pain in his head.

"Where is it, that lofty sky that I did not know till now, but saw today?" was his first thought.

"And I did not know this suffering either," he thought. "Yes, I did not know anything, anything at all till now.

But where am I?"

He listened and heard the sound of approaching horses, and  oices speaking French.

He opened his eyes.

Abo e him again was the same lofty sky with clouds that had risen and were floating still higher, and between them gleamed blue infinity.

He did not turn his head and did not see those who, judging by the sound of hoofs and  oices, had ridden up and stopped near him.

It was Napoleon accompanied by two aides de camp.

Bonaparte riding o er the battlefield had gi en final orders to strengthen the batteries firing at the Augesd Dam and was looking at the killed and wounded left on the field.

"Fine men!" remarked Napoleon, looking at a dead Russian grenadier, who, with his face buried in the ground and a blackened nape, lay on his stomach with an already stiffened arm flung wide.

"The ammunition for the guns in position is exhausted, Your Majesty," said an adjutant who had come from the batteries that were firing at Augesd.

"Ha e some brought from the reser e," said Napoleon, and ha ing gone on a few steps he stopped before Prince Andrew, who lay on his back with the flagstaff that had been dropped beside him. (The flag had already been taken by the French as a trophy.)

"That's a fine death!" said Napoleon as he gazed at Bolkonski.

Prince Andrew understood that this was said of him and that it was Napoleon who said it.

He heard the speaker addressed as Sire.

But he heard the words as he might ha e heard the buzzing of a fly.

Not only did they not interest him, but he took no notice of them and at once forgot them.

His head was burning, he felt himself bleeding to death, and he saw abo e him the remote, lofty, and e erlasting sky.

He knew it was Napoleon     his hero     but at that moment Napoleon seemed to him such a small, insignificant creature compared with what was passing now between himself and that lofty infinite sky with the clouds flying o er it.

At that moment it meant nothing to him who might be standing o er him, or what was said of him  he was only glad that people were standing near him and only wished that they would help him and bring him back to life, which seemed to him so beautiful now that he had today learned to understand it so differently.

He collected all his strength, to stir and utter a sound.

He feebly mo ed his leg and uttered a weak, sickly groan which aroused his own pity.

"Ah! He is ali e," said Napoleon. "Lift this young man up and carry him to the dressing station."

Ha ing said this, Napoleon rode on to meet Marshal Lannes, who, hat in hand, rode up smiling to the Emperor to congratulate him on the  ictory.

Prince Andrew remembered nothing more  he lost consciousness from the terrible pain of being lifted onto the stretcher, the jolting while being mo ed, and the probing of his wound at the dressing station.

He did not regain consciousness till late in the day, when with other wounded and captured Russian officers he was carried to the hospital.

During this transfer he felt a little stronger and was able to look about him and e en speak.

The first words he heard on coming to his senses were those of a French con oy officer, who said rapidly 

"We must halt here  the Emperor will pass here immediately  it will please him to see these gentlemen prisoners."

"There are so many prisoners today, nearly the whole Russian army, that he is probably tired of them," said another officer.

"All the same!

They say this one is the commander of all the Emperor Alexander's Guards," said the first one, indicating a Russian officer in the white uniform of the Horse Guards.

Bolkonski recognized Prince Repnin whom he had met in Petersburg society.

Beside him stood a lad of nineteen, also a wounded officer of the Horse Guards.

Bonaparte, ha ing come up at a gallop, stopped his horse.

"Which is the senior?" he asked, on seeing the prisoners.

They named the colonel, Prince Repnin.

"You are the commander of the Emperor Alexander's regiment of Horse Guards?" asked Napoleon.

"I commanded a squadron," replied Repnin.

"Your regiment fulfilled its duty honorably," said Napoleon.

"The praise of a great commander is a soldier's highest reward," said Repnin.

"I bestow it with pleasure," said Napoleon. "And who is that young man beside you?"

Prince Repnin named Lieutenant Sukhtelen.

After looking at him Napoleon smiled.

"He's  ery young to come to meddle with us."

"Youth is no hindrance to courage," muttered Sukhtelen in a failing  oice.

"A splendid reply!" said Napoleon. "Young man, you will go far!"

Prince Andrew, who had also been brought forward before the Emperor's eyes to complete the show of prisoners, could not fail to attract his attention.

Napoleon apparently remembered seeing him on the battlefield and, addressing him, again used the epithet "young man" that was connected in his memory with Prince Andrew.

"Well, and you, young man," said he. "How do you feel, mon bra e?"

Though fi e minutes before, Prince Andrew had been able to say a few words to the soldiers who were carrying him, now with his eyes fixed straight on Napoleon, he was silent.... So insignificant at that moment seemed to him all the interests that engrossed Napoleon, so mean did his hero himself with his paltry  anity and joy in  ictory appear, compared to the lofty, equitable, and kindly sky which he had seen and understood, that he could not answer him.

E erything seemed so futile and insignificant in comparison with the stern and solemn train of thought that weakness from loss of blood, suffering, and the nearness of death aroused in him.

Looking into Napoleon's eyes Prince Andrew thought of the insignificance of greatness, the unimportance of life which no one could understand, and the still greater unimportance of death, the meaning of which no one ali e could understand or explain.

The Emperor without waiting for an answer turned away and said to one of the officers as he went 

"Ha e these gentlemen attended to and taken to my bi ouac  let my doctor, Larrey, examine their wounds.

Au re oir, Prince Repnin!" and he spurred his horse and galloped away.

His face shone with self satisfaction and pleasure.

The soldiers who had carried Prince Andrew had noticed and taken the little gold icon Princess Mary had hung round her brother's neck, but seeing the fa or the Emperor showed the prisoners, they now hastened to return the holy image.

Prince Andrew did not see how and by whom it was replaced, but the little icon with its thin gold chain suddenly appeared upon his chest outside his uniform.

"It would be good," thought Prince Andrew, glancing at the icon his sister had hung round his neck with such emotion and re erence, "it would be good if e erything were as clear and simple as it seems to Mary.

How good it would be to know where to seek for help in this life, and what to expect after it beyond the gra e!

How happy and calm I should be if I could now say  'Lord, ha e mercy on me!'... But to whom should I say that?

Either to a Power indefinable, incomprehensible, which I not only cannot address but which I cannot e en express in words     the Great All or Nothing " said he to himself, "or to that God who has been sewn into this amulet by Mary!

There is nothing certain, nothing at all except the unimportance of e erything I understand, and the greatness of something incomprehensible but all important."

The stretchers mo ed on.

At e ery jolt he again felt unendurable pain  his fe erishness increased and he grew delirious.

 isions of his father, wife, sister, and future son, and the tenderness he had felt the night before the battle, the figure of the insignificant little Napoleon, and abo e all this the lofty sky, formed the chief subjects of his delirious fancies.

The quiet home life and peaceful happiness of Bald Hills presented itself to him.

He was already enjoying that happiness when that little Napoleon had suddenly appeared with his unsympathizing look of shortsighted delight at the misery of others, and doubts and torments had followed, and only the hea ens promised peace.

Toward morning all these dreams melted and merged into the chaos and darkness of unconciousness and obli ion which in the opinion of Napoleon's doctor, Larrey, was much more likely to end in death than in con alescence.

"He is a ner ous, bilious subject," said Larrey, "and will not reco er."

And Prince Andrew, with others fatally wounded, was left to the care of the inhabitants of the district.

N
